Master’s Project
2
Abstract
The aim of the project is to gain insights into the behavior of Estimation of Distribution
Algorithms (EDA’s) using island models and the causes of said behavior. Particularly when
varying population size, problem size, number of islands (cluster size), and migration interval.
To achieve this a highly modular framework was developed in C++ to facilitate the
large scale evaluations of EDA’s with various topologies and migration strategies using high
performance computing techniques. An implementation of a data parsing framework was in-
cluded, made in C#, to produce visualizations, tables, and statistical tests on large amounts
of data.
The framework has been used to evaluate UMDA and cGA on known bit-string problems
such as OneMax, LeadingOnes and JumpK. The algorithms were also evaluated on TwoPeaks
[4.1.4] and specific instances of the K-Sat problem encoded into bit-strings [4.1.6].
The
evaluation identifies statistically sound improvements for UMDA on cGA when using island
models of a factor up to 11 1 on OneMax and up to a factor of 7 2 on LeadingOnes. It has
also been found that through proper configuration of an island model, difficult problems for
island models such as TwoPeaks can improve using island models by a factor of 7 3.
Several patterns in behavior have been observed through the experiments conducted
in this thesis. Through experimentation on OneMax using cGA it was discovered that the
amount of Genetic Drift was lessened by diversity enforcing migration strategies (MuSample
and Probability migration), but harder to get rid of once present 4.
Further through experimentation on TwoPeaks it was concluded that the migration
intervals size could affect diversity and control alignment of the nodes’ probability vectors
to avoid conflicts between nodes. That same alignment is what made genetic drift more
difficult to get rid of. Alignment could also happen in Groups of a topology, given that the
topology had low enough connectivity. This gives diversity in the cluster without conflict
within the groups.
Testing on simple problems (OneMax, LeadingOnes) it was observed that with larger
population sizes an Elitist migration strategy (Sample migration) made improvements through
enforcing all nodes’ participation in optimizing from the point with highest fitness. For more
complex problems (JumpK, TwoPeaks, MaxSat) pairing this with diversity enforced through
topology leads to fast progress in groups within the topology while still keeping diversity on
a larger scale.
1The speedup found by comparing UMDA (λ = 17, µ = 1) using MuSample migration strategy with
Complete topology and cluster size 16, against UMDA with the same cluster size but NoMigration. The
speedup was measured in Largest fitness evaluations. For definition see section 7, for results see section
7.1.1.3
2The speedup found by comparing cGA (K = 99) using Sample migration strategy with Complete
topology and cluster size 16, against cGA with the same cluster size but NoMigration. The speedup was
measured in Largest fitness evaluations. For definition see section 7, for results see section 7.2.2.2
3The speedup found by comparing cGA K = 101 using Sample migration on the Complete topology with
cluster size 16, against cGA with the same cluster size but NoMigration. The speedup was measured in
Largest fitness evaluations. For definition see 7, for results see 7.2.3.2
4The migrations make genetic drift only possible once all connected notes agree on it, but once all agree
on it, getting rid of it also requires all nodes to agree
Page 1 of 107
==PAGE:8==
Master’s Project
Testing on JumpK also showed a balance between the benefits of diversity and the
benefit of sharing information quickly as a balance in connectivity in the topology gave the
best results.
Using UMDA on OneMax also showed the importance of selecting a good migration
interval to a problem, as the migrations differ in effectiveness depending on the progress of
the Algorithm at specific timings.
3
Introduction
3.1
Repository and Data
The software developed for this project can be found at:
https://github.com/JacobHej/MasterThesisEDAIslandModels
The Data we gathered can be found at:
https://drive.google.com/drive/folders/1W8LuP_t58DeVnmk551V41UQ5pnIvqsAL?usp=sharing
3.2
Aim of the project
EA’s (Evolutionary Algorithms) are a very well researched form of optimization algo-
rithms. They are used as a way to solve some of the more difficult problems such as the
NP-Complete problems. One way researchers have made them more effective is through
parallelization, running multiple algorithms at the same time. Further even the parallel al-
gorithms can then communicate findings. A well known way of doing this is the island model.
Here multiple EA’s run in parallel and share their solutions with each other at appropriate
points.
Another form of optimization algorithm are the EDA’s (Estimation of Distribution Al-
gorithm). They can also be parallelized for optimization, but the idea of communication
between them is relatively unexplored.
The aim of this project is to emperically evaluate the effects of combining EDA’s with
the island model. Both EDA’s and island models exist in many variations so for this project
a small sample of the possible combinations have been selected through the selection of 2
algorithms, 4 topologies and 3 migration strategies. The topologies and migration strategies
together with a migration interval, which is tested for later, make our island model.
Another aim of this project is do develop a modular performant software framework,
that allows for conducting experiments with various combinations of EDA’s, topologies, and
migration strategies in a high performance compute cluster. The framework should be able
to capture relevant metrics used for analysis and parse the data to produce visualizations.
Furthermore the framework should allow for easy implementation of different migration
strategies, topologies, and EDA’s.
Many other ways of creating an island model exists, but for this project the following
types are covered:
Page 2 of 107

Related works
Algorithms:
• UMDA
• cGA
Topologies:
• Complete
• Torus
• Circle/Ring
• One-Directional Star
Migration Strategies:
• Sample
• MuSample
• Probability migration
The algorithms were chosen for being some of the simplest (Univariate) and well re-
searched EDA’s while still having different functionality. The topologies were chosen for
varying degrees of connectivity, with the One-Directional Star chosen to test a topology
where some islands are unaffected by migration. The migration strategies were chosen for
their different functionalities. In total, 24 combinations can be made from these, all of whom
will be tested on 5 different bit-string problems.
Chosen problems: OneMax, LeadingOnes, TwoPeaks, JumpK, and MaxSat
The problems OneMax and LeadingOnes were chosen, as they are well-researched bench-
marks with known optimization times. TwoPeaks was selected to investigate conflict between
islands in the island model.
JumpK to test performance on jump functions and traps.
MaxSat was chosen as a test on how EDA’s using the island models would perform on an
NP-Complete problem compared to the EDA’s alone.
With this selection of different problems, migration strategies and topologies, this report
covers the impacts of EDA’s using island models for functionally different configurations, on
various benchmark problems. The purpose of this is to gain insights into how these different
migration strategies behave on benchmark problems, which each represent elements that
might be found in search spaces of NP-Complete problems.Furthermore we evaluate these
EDA’s using the island model on an NP-Complete problem. Analysing these behaviors and
testing empirically how much of an improvement can be achieved is the purpose of this
project.
3.3
Related works
This section provides an overview of key results in the area of speeding up EDA’s through
island models. It covers work similar to ours, interesting applications and other ways to use
extra computational power to increase performance.
Island models is one way to utilize more computational power in parallel, but it is im-
portant to note what other ways the computational power can increase the performance. We
found two interesting reports on the matter.
[1]"Parallel Estimation of Distribution Algorithms". Madera, Alba and Ochoa offer in-
teresting ways to parallelize EDA’s in ways to speed up its running time. They also give
brief insight into some of the choices one needs to make when designing an island model for
Page 3 of 107
==PAGE:10==
Master’s Project
3.3
Related works
an EDA and do a small test of the speedup on OneMax using an island model EDA.
[2]"Design of Parallel Estimation of Distribution Algorithms". Ocenasek, Cantú-Paz,
Pelikan and Schwarz, in this paper, go into depth on the different ways EDA’s can be paral-
lelised to achieve speedups in some of the more computationally heavy parts of the common
EDA. This includes Sampling, Evaluation of samples and updating the probability vector.
By running certain parts of the algorithm in parallel a significant speedup can be
achieved, but we of course mean to investigate how island models affect the performance
and workings of the EDA. We found three sources detailing specific distributed EDA models.
[3]"A Parallel Island Model for Estimation of Distribution Algorithms". Madera, Alba
and Ochoa investigate a single form of distributed UMDA (dUMDA) and test it on multiple
test functions in both the discrete and continous domain. They provide an in-depth inves-
tigation into how this single form of distributed UMDA, numerically, outperforms existing
approaches.
[4]"Migration of probability models instead of individuals: An alternative when apply-
ing the island model to EDAs". Yao, Burke, Lozano, Smith, MereloGuervos, Bullinaria,
Rowe, Tino, Kaban and Schwefel propose migrating probability vectors instead of solutions
when applying the island model to EDAs. They perform several experiments on a multitude
of test-functions evaluating how this approach compares to the sequential running of the
UMDA.
[5]"Migration of probabilistic models for island-based bivariate EDA algorithm". Schwarz,
Jaros and Ocenasek propose a similar island model to that of [4], but extend it to work on
bivariate models.
The reports above all investigate the different approaches to distributed island mod-
els and evaluate their performance compared to the sequential running of EDA’s. In this
project the purpose is to compare different methods with each other and examine their work-
ings. A similar evaluation was done in "Distributed Estimation of Distribution Algorithms
for continuous optimization: How does the exchanged information influence their behavior?".
[6]"Distributed Estimation of Distribution Algorithms for continuous optimization: How
does the exchanged information influence their behavior?".
Santiago Muelas, Alexander
Mendiburu, Antonio LaTorre and Josè-Marìa extensively covered the performances of differ-
ent combinations of population size, islands, period, topology and model in this paper. They
analyse which configurations do the best, what parameters mattered the most and made fan-
tastic plots on how different variables interact with each other in terms of performance.
What we aim to do differently is both what exact models and fitness functions we test
on, and the depth we go into each test. We will focus less on the importance of each variable
and the numerical improvements and more on the workings of each variable and how it affects

3.4
Structure of the report
The report is structured as follows: In section 4) The theoretical backgrounds is covered.
In section 5) we cover the design decisions, scoping of the software framework, and analysis
of requirements. In section 6) the implementation of the framework is covered. In section
7) The experiments for UMDA, and cGA using island models are covered. In section 8) The
conclusion and findings are covered, and in section 9) A List of further works can be found.
4
Theoretical Background
This section will cover the theoretical background necessary for the design, implemen-
tation and evaluation as well as some side details.
4.1
SearchSpace
The search space for a problem is a set of all the possible solutions to a problem [7].
The fitness of a solution in this set is a number showing how good a solution is. The fitness
of a solution is comparable to the fitness of other solutions, and in some problem instances
we might know the maximum fitness. To evaluate a solutions fitness a fitness function is
applied.
To visualize a search space one might use the fitness as a y-axis and the dimensions of
the problem as the x-axis. A 1-Dimensional problem for example could be visualized like
seen in figure 2.
Figure 2: A figure found at
https://www.researchgate.net/figure/state-space-landscape-Lazebnik-2018_
fig3_337347748 visualizing the search space concept.
Page 5 of 107
==PAGE:12==
Master’s Project
4.1
SearchSpace
A given solution would be a point on said graph and its closest points are part of its
"neighbourhood". From the shape of its neighborhood we call it different things. If its the
highest point in its neighborhood we call it a local maxima. If its the lowest, a local minima.
If the neighborhood is flat we call it a plataeu.
There also exists a global maxima and
minima, which are the very highest and very lowest point in the entire search space. Some
problems can also have multiple global maxima with the same fitness, such as TwoPeaks.
Finding the global maxima or a point close in fitness is the goal of both EA’s and EDA’s,
but local maxima that aren’t the global maxima tend to fool algorithms, especially if the
surrounding points have a rather low fitness. We sometimes also call the maxima for optima.
4.1.1
Bit Strings
A bit string x is a list of 0’s and 1’s xi. The search space for bit-string solutions is any
combinations of 0’s and 1’s with a length n determined by the problem, most often referred
to as problem size. The size of the search space grows exponentionally with problem size
as each added bit doubles the amount of possible solutions. This growth is why brute force
algorithms are disregarded as ways of finding the global optima. [7].
4.1.2
OneMax
The goal of OneMax is to maximize the amount of 1’s in the bit-string. With its fitness
value being the amount of 1’s. Formally OneMax is defined:[8]
OneMax(x1, x2, .., xn) =
n
X
i=1
xi
We call OneMax simple, since the goal is so easily achieved. There is only one local optima,
all bits attribute to fitness from the beginning to the end and there is no dependency between
them. The simple nature of OneMax leads more greedy, aggressive and elitist algorithms to
perform better [9].
We find it important to note the difficulty of progression as fitness goes up. To progress
from one fitness to a higher one, the bits set to 0 must be flipped and as the fitness goes up,
the amount of 0’s go down. The progress is also somewhat slowed by the increasing chance
of flipping a 1 to a 0.
4.1.3
LeadingOnes
LeadingOnes is a function with the same goal as OneMax, but its fitness is defined
as the amount of consecutive 1’s from the most significant bit. Formally LeadingOnes is
defined[8]:
LeadingOnes(x1, x2, .., xn) =
n
X
i=1
iY
j=1
xj
LeadingOnes is still a relatively simple problem, but with the new dependency for each bit
of all previous bits the progress is limited. While we might flip 10 0’s to 1’s thereby getting
Page 6 of 107
==PAGE:13==
Master’s Project
4.1
SearchSpace
closer to the goal, the fitness only goes up for the consecutive bits. At the same time the
difficulty of getting higher fitness does not increase as the amount of 0’s decline [9].
4.1.4
TwoPeaks
The TwoPeaks function is a problem we’ve chosen with the intention of testing on a
problem which gets harder with the island model.
To the best of our knowledge it has
not been researched before. Its goal is to maximize either consecutive 0’s from the least
significant bit (this is also called TrailingZeros) or consecutive 1’s from the most significant
bit (this is also called LeadingOnes). The concept comes from FourPeaks [10]. Formally
TrailingZeros and TwoPeaks is defined:
TrailingZeros(x1, x2, .., xn) =
n
X
i=1
iY
j=1
xn−j
TwoPeaks(x) = max(LeadingOnes(x), TrailingZeros(x))
Jörg Lässig and Dirk Sudholt had a somewhat similar function called LOLZ (short for
LeadingOnesLeadingZeroes) [11]. Two main differences exist. The first is what we’d like to
call point of conflict. For LOLZ from the very beginning two algorithms can work on bit x0
and disagree on wether it should be 0 or 1. With TwoPeaks the point of conflict is moved to
xn/2 where the algorithms solving LeadingOnes and the ones solving TrailingZeroes should
meet on average. Some conflict also occours between the two sides before this point as the
bits, their respective functions do not work on yet are as likely to be 1 as 0. The other
main difference is that LOLZ has a cut-off point z whereafter only LeadingOnes can increase
fitness. At this point the behavior between TwoPeaks and LOLZ completely differ as conflict
continues for TwoPeaks and LOLZ ends up with trapped algorithms.
4.1.5
JumpK
Formally JumpK is defined:[8]
Jumpk(x) =
(
OneMax(x) + k
if OneMax(x) ≤n −k or OneMax(x) = n
n −OneMax(x)
otherwise
The goal of JumpK is to maximize the amount of 1’s in the bistring and does so in the same
way as OneMax, except for a jump of size k when nearing the maximum amount of bits.
The easiest way to explain the fitness is through a visualization in figure 3

fitness as the y-axis. Source: [12]
The gradiant of the fitness within the jump leads algorithms towards a smaller amount
of 1’s. This increases the difficulity for algorithms that take smaller steps, and forces most
algorithms to try and get from n-k 1’s to n 1’s in one iteration5.
4.1.6
MaxSat
The MaxSat problem has the goal of maximizing the amount of satisfied Clauses for
a formula given in Conjunctive normal form (CNF for short).
A formula in CNF is a
conjunction of one or more clauses, each of which contains a dis-junction of literals which are
boolean variables or their negated forms. For our purpose the conjunction does not matter
as we count the amount of satisfied Clauses which we will describe as a list C = C0, C1..Cm.
We define the satisfaction of boolean assignment x on a clause Ci to be Ci(x) which gives us
the formal definition of MaxSat:
MaxSat(x) =
m
X
i=1
Ci(x)[13]
In MaxSat the bits are not independent as the clauses force relations upon them. This makes
it a very hard problem to solve especially for the UniVariate algorithms we work with in the
report. Much of the complexity of the problem comes with this dependency as it creates
many local maxima where to satisfy a new clause other clauses might become unsatisfied
and other bits need to be flipped to make them satisfied again. The problem of determining
if there exists a boolean assignment that satisfies all Clauses is also deemed NP-Complete
[14].
When all clauses are of the same length k the form it takes is called k −CNF and
the problem of satisfiability on it is the k −SAT problem [12]. When talking about K-
Sat, the phase transition of the problem is an important concept. The phase transition is
5Going from one solution to another in one iteration is called jumping. It is more commonly used when
the two solutions are far apart in the search space

4.2
EDA
some point in the ratio between boolean variables and amount of clauses where the sat-
isfiability of the problems drop rapidly. Just before this point the difficulty of determin-
ing a satisfying assignment goes up [15]. We have selected instances of the problem from
https://www.cs.ubc.ca/ hoos/SATLIB/benchm.html (visited 07/06/2024), where we have
selected only satisfiable problems near the phase transition so we could have varying diffi-
culty of the problems.
4.2
EDA
EDA’s (Estimation of Distribution Algorithms) are a form of optimization algorithms
that progress through incremental changes to a probabilistic model [16]. They generally
work in 3 steps:
• Generate solutions from probabilistic model
• Evaluate solutions with some fitness function
• Update model solutions based on evaluation
The purpose is to iterativly improve the quality of the generated solutions until we find some
local maxima, hopefully the global maxima.
The exact probabilistic model changes from algorithm to algorithm and problem to
problem. We use two Univariate algorithms, UMDA and CGA on bitstring problems. Uni-
variate means the algorithm assumes no variables to be dependent in any way. It lets each
variable be sampled independently from the other and updates each variables probability
independently. Working on bitstrings the simplest Univariate model is for each bit to have
a probability to be 1 or 0, making the model a list of probabilities the same length as the
bitstring. We call this list of probabilities, the probability vector.
Another note is that our EDA’s are increment based and not population based [16].
Diversity
For evolutionary algorithms the diversity is the variance in solutions in its pop-
ulation [16][17]. This diversity can play a part in crossovers and also shows how exploratory
an algorithm is and can lead to improvements in runtime for more complex problems [18].
For EDA’s the diversity comes in what solutions are generated and the variance in them.
With a probabilistic model, the diversity of the generated solutions come from how close the
probabilities of the bits are to 50%. The further away a probability is the less chance there
is of two generated solutions having different values at that bit.
Genetic Drift
For EDA’s a term known as genetic drift is quite important. Genetic drift
is a tendency for EDA’s probabilities to move away from 50% over time. This effect will
occur whenever the probability’s change from fitness is low. This can be when a bit does
not contribute to fitness yet, such as the least significant bit in the start of LeadingOnes or
when a bit has low contribution to the fitness compared to other effects. The exact reasons
for Genetic Drift differs between our two algorithms so we will go over it in their respective
sections. [19].
Page 9 of 107
==PAGE:16==
Master’s Project

EDA
4.2.1
UMDA
UMDA is an EDA with two variables, µ and λ. They control how many solutions are
generated from the probabilistic model and how many of the best solution get to affect the
probabilistic model. Formally the definition is (algorithm 1 [20]):
Algorithm 1 UMDA
1: t ←0 , pt,0 ←pt,1 ←pt,2 ←· · · ←pt,n ←1
2
2: while termination criterion not met do
3:
for j ∈{1, .., λ} do
4:
for i ∈{1, .., n} do
5:
xj,i ←1 with prob pt,i and xj,i ←0 otherwise
6:
end for
7:
Pt ←Pt ∪xj
8:
end for
9:
Sort individuals in Pt descending by fitness (such that f(x0) ≥· · · ≥f(xn))
10:
for i ∈{1, .., n} do
11:
pt+1,i ←
Pµ
j=1 xj,i
µ
12:
Restrict pt+1,i to be within [lowerBound, upperBound]
13:
end for
14:
t ←t + 1
15: end while
Each round the algorithm sample λ individuals, selects the µ best and averages out their
bits to set the probabilities for the next round. This algorithm is known to lose diversity
[21] [16] which has implications for certain problems such as JumpK.
Genetic Drift
For UMDA genetic drift occurs as the µ selected from the sampled popula-
tion does not match the current probability. As we are looking at bits that do not affect the
fitness we can assume that the samples follow the Binomial distribution. Looking at table
1, we see two trends. One is that as p gets further from 0.5, the probability of X matching
the expected value goes up. This means that as a probability strays the chance of movement
decreases. The other thing we note is that the ratio between X > 1000 · p and X < 1000 · p
changes as p gets higher and lower. From this it can be observed that the probability tends
to move further away from 50% rather than closer.

4.2.1.1
Theoretical runtimes
This section briefly covers some of the known Theoretical runtimes of UMDA on different
bitString problems problems.
OneMax
For OneMax two different population sizes exists that each are a local maxima.
This is called bi-modality. The first comes at µ = clog(n) for some constant c > 0 and
λ = c′µ for some constant c′ >= 1. The expected runtime of this population size is O(µn).
The second population size comes at µ = c√nlog(n) for some constant c > 0 and λ = c′µ
for some constant c′ >= 1. The expected runtime of this population size is O(µ√n) [20].
LeadingOnes
In "A Simplified Run Time Analysis of the Univariate Marginal Distribution
Algorithm on LeadingOnes" [22], it was found that with µ = nlog(n) and λ = (Θ(1) + 1)µ
the expected runningtime of UMDA on leadingOnes becomes O(n
λ
log(λ/µ)).
4.2.2
CGA
CGA is an EDA with one variable K which represents the simulated Population size.
It works by sampling two solutions, grading which one is best and then for each index add
the difference between the best and the worst divided by K. This means each iteration it
takes steps of size 1/K on each bit where the two solutions differ. More formally it works as
shown in algorithm 2[23].
Page 11 of 107
==PAGE:18==
Master’s Project
4.2
EDA
Algorithm 2 CGA
1: t ←0 , pt,0 ←pt,1 ←pt,2 ←· · · ←pt,n ←1
2
2: while termination criterion not met do
3:
for i ∈{1, .., n} do
4:
xi ←1 with prob pt,i and xi ←0 otherwise
5:
yi ←1 with prob pt,i and yi ←0 otherwise
6:
end for
7:
if f(x) < f(y) then
8:
swap x and y
9:
end if
10:
for i ∈{1, .., n} do
11:
pt+1,i ←pt+1,i + (xi −yi)/K
12:
Restrict pt+1,i to be within [lowerBound, upperBound]
13:
end for
14:
t ←t + 1
15: end while
Genetic Drift
Genetic Drift for CGA comes from a larger amount of movement for proba-
bilities around 50% than those closer to 0% or 100%. To move, the two samples have to differ
on a given point. We can calculate the chance of differing samples with certain probabilities
using the binomial formula again. With a probability of 50% the chance of movement is
50%. At 10% probability the chance becomes 18% and at 1% probability it becomes 1.9%.
4.2.2.1
Theoretical runtimes
This section will quickly cover some of the expected runtimes of the CGA.
OneMax
"It is known that the cGA and the Univariate Marginal Distribution Algorithm
(UMDA), a related algorithm whose population size is called λ, run in expected time O(n
log n)"[23]. This happens when K = Θ(nlog(n)). Furthermore cGA on OneMax shows a
bimodality with population size log(n) similar to UMDA.
LeadingOnes
Due to CGA’s close relation to UMDA and other genetic algorithms we
expect the runtime of it to be equal to their runtimes. Thus we expect the runtime of CGA
to be O(n2log(n)).
Jumpk
For jumpk, the time it takes to complete scales with problem size and jump size.
Scaling jumpsize and population size with problem size, Benjamin Doerr got a running time
of O(µn1.5 + n3.1+ϵ) [24].

4.3
Island Model
4.3
Island Model
One way to increase performance of genetic algorithms and EDA’s is to run multiple
instances of the algorithm of choice in parallel. One way to further increase the performance
is to let these parallel runs communicate with each other. For genetic algorithms one general
method of doing this is the Island model where multiple populations evolve independently
and then exchange solutions sometimes. This exchange is referred to as migration. Here three
factors are important. One is the frequency of migration. In this report we work with a
static migration interval (Mi). The Mi value determines how many iterations the algorithms
run independently before all doing a migration round. Secondly the topology also called
the shape of the network. This topology determines what populations get to migrate to
where. When talking about topology one important factor is its connectivity, meaning how
strongly every node is connected to every other. Lastly how migration is done is important.
When working with Genetic Algorithms the method rarely differs from migrate a select set
of individuals, but with EDA’s a lot more ways of migrating present themselves.[16] [25]
Clusters, nodes and groups
In this report we use different terminology to refer to the
islands. When we refer to a node we are talking about an island running the algorithm. The
cluster is all the nodes on which we run. So a cluster of 16 means we had 16 algorithms
running at the same time. A group is a subset of the cluster, often closely connected through
the topology.
4.3.1
Topologies
This section covers the topologies we’ve used. We’ve picked Complete Torus and Circle
for varying levels of connectivity, and we’ve picked a one-directional Star topology to test
the effects of having nodes that don’t get influenced.

Figure 4: Visual representations of the chosen topologies
Complete
[26] The Complete topology is simple. All nodes are connected to each other
bi-directionally. This makes the max distance between two nodes 1. It has the strongest
connectivity possible. See figure 4a for a visualization.
Page 13 of 107
==PAGE:20==
Master’s Project
4.3
Island Model
Torus
[26] The torus topology is a wrapping grid of nodes each connected with their 4
neighbours. A visual representation of a 4x5 Torus can be seen in figure 4b.
Of our 4 topologies Torus has the second highest connectivity with a maximum length
between nodes on a x by y Torus being x
2 + y
2.
Circle
[26] More commonly known as the Ring topology, the Circle places all nodes in
a circle and lets each node communicate with their two neighbours. In a one-directional
variant they all migrate to one neighbor in the same direction around the circle, though
we’ve gone with the bi-directional circle. Of our three chosen connectivity examples Circle
has the lowest connectivity, with a maximum length between nodes of n
2. See figure 4c for a
visualization.
As a note, K-Ring/K-Circle also exists where the circle is allowed to communicate with
neighbors K edges away. Circle topology is equal to KCircle with K = 1.
Star
The last of the topologies in this report is the star topology where 1 center node is
connected to all other nodes and all other nodes are only connected to the center node. A
two-directional version of this makes all migration happen through the center node. In this
report, a one-directional version was chosen where all migration is directed to the center
node. The benefit of this is that all the outer nodes do not get influenced and thus the
diversity of the cluster is kept. See figure 4d for a visualization.
4.3.2
Migration Strategies
This section covers the migration strategies we’ve used. We have a total of 3, two of
which communicate sampled populations in different ways (Sample migration and MuSample
migration) and 1 which samples the probability vector (Probability migration). Pseudo code
will be provided for the migration strategies. In the pseudo-code Migrate(x) will send x to
all connected nodes and return the collection of both x as well as all received xmigrated from
connected nodes.
For Sample migration and MuSample migration the exact implementations do not exist
in current literature to the best of our knowledge. However both strategies are based on the
general principles of the island model described in "A Parallel Island Model for Estimation
of Distribution Algorithms"[27].
4.3.2.1
Sample Migration
The idea of sample migration is to see all migrated samples as one big population and
update the probability vector with the best samples overall. This should all nodes take the
best step at the cost of diversity. It is inspired by migration in (µ,λ)EA’s where the migrated
individuals gets integrated into the population according to fitness [27].
UMDA
Sample migration for UMDA takes all incoming migrated data together with its
own samples and sees this as one giant population. Then samples the µ best as normal and
makes the next iteration’s probability vector as normal. If Pfullt = Pt ∪Pmigrated represents
Page 14 of 107
==PAGE:21==
Master’s Project
4.3
Island Model
the current population combined with the received migrated populations and xfullj,i is the
i’th bit on the j’th solution in that combined population, then formally we get:
Algorithm 3 Sample migration UMDA
1: for j ∈{1, .., λ} do
2:
for i ∈{1, .., n} do
3:
xj,i ←1 with prob pt,i and xj,i ←0 otherwise
4:

5:

6: end for

µ

12: end for
cGA
For cGA, the migrated data is multiple pairs of samples. We select the sample with
the highest fitness and let its corresponding pair update the probability vector as normal.
If Pfullt = Pt ∪Pmigrated represents the current population combined with the received mi-
grated populations, xfullj,i is the i’th bit on the j’th solution in that combined population
and each value xfullj knows its corresponding yfullj, then formally we get:
Algorithm 4 Sample migration cGA
1: for i ∈{1, .., n} do
2:
xi ←1 with prob pt,i and xi ←0 otherwise
3:
yi ←1 with prob pt,i and yi ←0 otherwise
4: end for
5: Pfullt ←Migrate(x,y)
6: Sort individuals in Pfullt descending by fitness (such that f(xfull0) ≥··· ≥f(xfulln))
7: x ←xfull0
8: y ←xfull0’s corresponding y.
9: for i ∈{1, .., n} do
10:
pt+1,i ←pt+1,i + (xi −yi)/K
11:
Restrict pt+1,i to be within [lowerBound, upperBound]
12: end for
4.3.2.2
MuSample Migration
MuSample migration cam from the idea of letting all connected nodes affect each other
as they would themselves on a normal iteration. The effect must then be averaged out to let
Page 15 of 107
==PAGE:22==
Master’s Project
4.3
Island Model
the algorithm run as normal. This should help keep diversity at the cost of slower progress.
UMDA
For UMDA the selection process happens before the migration, then upon re-
ceiving the µ best samples from each connected node the probability vector is updated as
normal, but with a larger selected population. If Pfullt = Pt ∪Pmigrated represents the
current population combined with the received migrated populations and xfullj,i is the i’th
bit on the j’th solution in that combined population, then formally we get:
Algorithm 5 MuSample migration UMDA
1: for j ∈{1, .., λ} do
2:
for i ∈{1, .., n} do
3:
xj,i ←1 with prob pt,i and xj,i ←0 otherwise
4:
end for
5:
Pt ←Pt ∪xj
6: end for
7: Sort individuals in Pt descending by fitness (such that f(x0) ≥· · · ≥f(xn))
8: Pfullt ←Migrate(P[0..µ])
9: for i ∈{1, .., n} do
10:
pt+1,i ←
Pm
j=1 xfullj,i
m
11:
Restrict pt+1,i to be within [lowerBound, upperBound]
12: end for

Cga receives m pairs of samples and then lets all pairs affect the probability vector
with
1
m of their normal strength. This idea of averaged out steps, comes from the idea of
weighted steps in cga [28, 29]. If Pfullt = Pt ∪Pmigrated represents the current population
combined with the received migrated populations, xfullj,i is the i’th bit on the j’th solution
in that combined population and each value xfullj knows its corresponding yfullj, then
formally we get:

Algorithm 6 MuSample migration cGA
1: for i ∈{1, .., n} do
2:
xi ←1 with prob pt,i and xi ←0 otherwise
3:
yi ←1 with prob pt,i and yi ←0 otherwise
4: end for
5: if f(x) < f(y) then
6:
swap x and y
7: end if
8: Pfullt ←Migrate(x,y)
9: Sort Pfullt into pairs such that xfulli+1 is xfulli’s corresponding yfulli for all even
values of i.
10: for i ∈{1, .., n} do
11:

j=1
(xfulli−xfulli+1)
m
12:
Restrict pt+1,i to be within [lowerBound, upperBound]
13: end for
4.3.2.3
Probability Migration
Probability migration happens by sending the probability vector rather than samples.
The incomming m probability vectors (P j
t,i for j = 0..m) and the current nodes own vector
are then averaged out [30]. For this report a variant of this employing variable β ∈0..1 [31].
β controls how much priority the current nodes vector takes over all incomming vectors.
Formally we get:
Algorithm 7 Probability migration
1: for i ∈{1, .., n} do
2:
pt+1,i ←pt,i · β + (1 −β) Pm
j=0
pj
t,i
m
3:
Restrict pt+1,i to be within [lowerBound, upperBound]
4: end for
For this report β was kept at 0.9, but testing different values for different algorithms,
topologies and problems might be interesting.
UMDA V cGA
Using a set β of 0.9, means that the migration strategy works quite
differently for the two Algorithms. This is due to how big the changes to probability vectors
between iterations are for the two algorithms. cGA does steps of size 1/K which compared
to a 10% change upon Probability migration can be quite small. For UMDA the reverse is
true as a normal iteration can provide a 100% change to the probability vector.
4.4
Statistical theory
Fischers test
Fischers test is used to test statistical significance normally employed when
the sample size is small where other tests like the Chi-Square test is not appropriate. It can
Page 17 of 107
==PAGE:24==
Master’s Project
be used to determine if there are non-random associations between two variables. Through
it a p-value is obtained which represents the likelihood of the two variables’ probabilities
being equal [32, 33]. It assumes binary variables and independence of observations meaning
one observation does not affect the next. In this report it, together with the Chi-Squared
test, will be used to test the significance of changes in success-rates when we use different
configurations of the island model. To do so we create a 2x2 matrix of success and failures
for the two configurations we want to compare and use R to perform the test.
Chi-square test
The Chi-square test is used to test statistical significance normally em-
ployed when the sample size is large. It can determine if there is a significant association
between two variables. Through it a p-value is obtained which represents the likelihood
of the two variables’ probabilities being equal. [34, 33]. It assumes binary variables and
independence of observations. To check if a dataset is large enough one can calculate the
expected frequencies and see if they are all above 5 [34].
Mann Whitney U test
The Mann Whitney U test is a hypothesis test that compares
two independent groups of data. In this report we will use it to test if the difference between
the medians of two groups of data is significant. The data we will use it for is the runtime,
measured in fitness evaluations of two configurations run multiple times. Using R we will
then perform the Mann Whitney U test on the two sets of data gathered. The p value it
produces will then represent the likelihood of the two configurations having equal average
running time [35]. The reason we use this test is it assumes no particular distribution shape
[36]. It also assumes independent groups of data and independent Observations, which our
method of acquiring data lives up to.
5
Design And Analysis
In this section we set out to analyze the technical requirements in order to scope the
framework needed to evaluate the selected estimation of distribution algorithms with island
models. The result of this will be conceptual solutions, which will be elaborated on in the
Implementation section.
5.1
Technical Analysis of requirements
In this section we will cover our analysis of technical requirements needed to scope the
development of frameworks and achieve the goals of the project. We will analyse the major
components and describe the abstract solution and requirements.
Tools and technologies
We have chosen to write the major components related to run-
ning and gathering data about island models and EDA’s in c++ as it is a high performant
language with inherent abstractions that allows us to build a high performing modular frame-
work. The framework will allow us to efficiently perform test on a large scale using DTU’s
HPC clusters using tested and validated scientific computing tools Like MPI[37]. For parsing
Page 18 of 107
==PAGE:25==
Master’s Project
5.1
Technical Analysis of requirements
data we have chosen to work with C# as it has built in libraries for easy sorting and group-
ing such as "Language Integrated Query" (LINQ) as well as easy parallelization through the
"Task Parallel Library" (TPL). For statistical analysis we have chosen the language R as it
comes with frameworks and API’s for most common statistical tests such as χ2, Fisher, and
Mann-Whitney U-test, all of which we have used for this project.
Project modules
To provide a clear architecture, clear separation of responsibilities and
clear data ownership we have structured the program into modules (logical abstraction).
• Algorithms, which encompasses the 1) EDAs, 2) logging and storage of information
about the state of the algorithms (used later for data parsing and evaluation), and 3)
coordination of data retention amongst parallel running instances during testing.
• Coordination, which encompasses 1) communication abstractions, 2) migration strate-
gies, 3) topologies, and 4) coordination of nodes/EDA’s running collectively in a cluster
with some topology.
• Fitness functions, which encompasses fitness function for each of the problems to be
investigated in this thesis.
• Common, which encompasses 1) IO of gathered data, 2) JSON utility, 3) Random
number generation, and 4) parsing of MaxSat instances.
• Testing, which encompasses testing of both the data parser and the implementation
of our framework for EDA’s and migration strategies.
• Data parsing, which encompasses 1) Parsing of the data and producing graphs, tables,
statistical summaries for Maple and Latex and 2) Code generation of R code to do
statistical calculations and generated Latex table
These modules allows for a logical abstraction of responsibilities and encapsulation of
functionality. This allows us to design the different frameworks/modules to suit different
needs as the implementation-wise can be independent of one another.
We designed the
Algorithms and Coordination modules to be highly performant, and through the Common
module produce data in a format that complies and couples with the Data parsing module
where flexibility was more important than performance.
The separation of running the
algorithms and parsing the data allowed us to do a variety of different analysis on the data
produced.
Coordinator and communication
For our testing purposes we need to be able to test
configurations (consisting of an EDA, migration strategy, fitness function, and topology)
which involves coordinating communication according to the topology, applying migrations
appropriately depending on the EDA and topology as well as evaluate the progress of an
EDA in relations to some fitness function. We also need to be able to communicate various
data types as decided by the select migration strategy and topology.
Page 19 of 107
==PAGE:26==
Master’s Project
5.1
Technical Analysis of requirements
Considering this dependency inversion becomes a critical abstraction such that each of
the components comprising a configuration can be supplied to some coordinator where the
individual implementations should not be known, however each component should expose
some interface.
This coordinator will be responsible for having all these components of a configuration
supplied and coordinate running the EDA with migration, checking if a run is finished by
some stopping criteria.
We also design a communication interface on top of MPI to provide a simple solution
for communicating, serializing, and deserializing datatypes.
Estimation of distribution algorithms
The Estimation of distribution algorithms should
be independent of the fitness function, topology and migration strategy, as we need to be
able to test each EDA with different migration strategies and topologies on different func-
tions. The EDA’s needs to support manipulations as described by the migration strategies.
Considering this the design should consist of an abstract base class describing basic func-
tionality and an interface for each EDA exposing the needed functionality for migration
strategies allowing for dependency inversion and a generic interface for the EDA’s, such that
each migration strategy can be used for any EDA.
Migration strategies
We need to be able to use different migration strategies for each
algorithm, and each migration strategy needs to be responsible for what data is communi-
cated, to which other nodes it is communicated and how to apply the change to an EDA.
Considering this the design should consist of an pure abstract base class describing an in-
terface for applying/doing migrations.
The Migration strategies should be aware of the
migration interval, the EDA and the topology, however not the specific implementations.
These should be designed considering dependency inversion as they will be supplied to some
coordinator that should not know about the specific implementation of neither the EDA nor
the Migration strategy.
Topologies
The topologies needs to describe given a cluster size only know at runtime
which nodes are neighbors for a given node, such that the migration strategies can apply the
strategy. These also need to be designed considering dependency inversion as the should be
supplied to some coordinator interchangeably.
Gathering Data
We need to be able to gather data about a run such that it can later
be evaluated and analysed. We have some requirements for which evaluations we need to
do, and the framework should also be open for extension such that gathering and evaluating
new information is possible.
To this end we need to be able to capture information about the run of an algorithm, and
identify which run, cluster and configuration it is part of. Considering this the design should
allow for algorithms to log information at each iteration and then along with identifying
information print this to be parsed and evaluated later. Due to limited space for storage
the design should also allow for truncation of data such that we do not store all information
Page 20 of 107
==PAGE:27==
Master’s Project
5.2
Architecture
about a run, this however needs to be done in a distributed manner as nodes in a cluster
needs to agree on which information they log to minimize linear interpolation later which
can skew the results. To this end we design a tool for such distributed data truncation.
We need to be able to parse the data and generate graphs, tables and R code for
statistical calculations. This is not performance critical and can be designed in a higher
level language like C#. We need to be able to group and cluster data by which experiment
it was part of and all aforementioned parameters, select the relevant metrics and generate
visual representations. Considering this the design should have some Parser that groups and
clusters data, a DataSelector that given some grouping can select relevant metrics, and some
generator component that can generate visual representations and R code.
Performance considerations
For testing purposes we need to run test on a large scale,
and given limited resources performance and efficiency is critical.
The design was done
with a focus on optimization techniques, particularly, such as memory locality, buffers to
avoid memory copying, instruction pipelining, and vectorization. For the simpler problems
where the runtime is not dominated by the fitness evaluations, we need sampling and pseudo
random number generation (PRNG) to be efficient, while having a reliable and high quality
PRNG . Two candidates (Xorshift128+[38] and Mersennes Twister[39]) were considered.
Xorshift128+ was faster and required less memory. Mersennes Twister had the benefit of a
higher period of 219937−1 compared to Xorshift128+’s 2128−1. For the purpose of this project
a period of 2128 −1 is enough. Lastly they both have systematic failures on LinearComp and
Matrix-Rank [40, 41, 42] for BigCrush TestU01. With all this in mind Xorshift128+ was
chosen.
The functions
To ensure the dependency inversion in fitness functions, they are all de-
signed to calculate the fitness on a section of a collection of samples. This also allows for
reference passing and avoid memory copying.
5.2
Architecture
5.2.1
EDA’s and coordination
As discussed in the previous section, the part of our solution that comprises the frame-
work used for testing is based around abstract classes, that present an interface which ex-
poses the functionality of each of these components (EDA, Migration strategy, Topology,
and Fitness function).
These are then passed to a Optimizer Coordinator class that manages running the algo-
rithm, applying the migration strategies in accordance with the topology and coordinating
with the other nodes in a cluster of when to stop as decided by some stopping criteria.
The communication is a exposed through a wrapper class simplifying the MPI interface
for our needs.
This allows us to implement a variety of fitness functions, EDA’s and migration strate-
gies as well as the needed topologies independent of the EDA and other components and
Page 21 of 107

Master’s Project
5.3
Repository overview
test any configuration with various parameter settings. And through the Common mod-
ule exposing logic for storing the data and metrics we can store data and later parse and
generate different visualizations. This crucially allows for new visualizations to be made if
needed that was not considered when data data was created, and further more allows for
easy extensions to storing additional data if new analysis techniques are needed.

6
Implementation
In this section the implementation of the framework for evaluating EDA’s and parsing
and visualizing data is covered. Implementation decisions for performance and accuracy will
also be explained in this section.
6.1
Sample and Probability distribution representation
Sample representation
We represent a sample as an array of uint16_t which always
takes the value 1 or 0. We chose this data type for the purpose of vectorization of the
sampling method as smaller data types like boolean’s or any 8-bit type was not supported by
the vector instructions we needed. This allowed performance optimizations while limiting the
memory used. We developed a custom type for this as we found that an array representation
has better performance than std::vector<t>. However we also wanted it to be dynamically
allocate-able at runtime with an unknown size and know its own length. For this the template
DynamicArray<t> was developed. It is a simple wrapper around a std::unique_ptr<t[]> for
clear data ownership, as well as some helper functions like concatenation. A set of samples
was finally represented by a class called SampleContainer which housed two instances of
DynamicArray<uint16_t>, one for the set of samples, and one for the fitness values of each
sample.
Probability distribution representation
For similar performance reasons we chose to
let the probability distribution be represented as a DynamicArray<uint16_t> which again
allowed for vectorization of the sampling method. This gave us an accuracy of +{
1
65535·2} =
0.000762951% in terms of representing the probabilities. This was insignificant compared to
the updates for the probability vector even for the largest population sizes worked with in
this thesis. Furthermore this was also evaluated against a type with larger decimal accuracy
(a Double) and yielded no change for the two algorithms in terms of performance for the
select problems of this thesis.
This was however also expected as these effects effectively are just a very slight change
to the population size with proper rounding for cGA. One should also note than when scaling
the population size with C′ · √n · ln(n) as for OneMax we also need to round the number to
get an integer value for λ and µ (for UMDA) which yields significantly larger changes to the
probability distribution as a result of updates compared to the accuracy of the probabilities.
We did implement the calculations for the probability distribution update using double
to minimize the loss of accuracy as a result of compounded rounding errors and only rounded
finally when storing the result.
We found this trade off to be reasonable for increased
performance.
Page 23 of 107
==PAGE:30==
Master’s Project
6.2
Pseudo random number generation
6.2
Pseudo random number generation
For random number generation we implemented a vectorized version of Xorshift128+, an
improved version of Xorshift128 that passes the tests from BigCrush and is a good quality
pseudo random number generator. In figure [7] we see the implementation of the PRNG
where we generate r_count random unsigned shorts into the buffer provided as a pointer.
Ensuring that the buffer has the capacity and r_count is divisible by 16 is handled outside
the method.
To achieve this vectorization we have 4 version of Xorshift128+ with 128
bits of randomness evaluated at once using vector instructions. We then store the 4 64-bit
unsigned integers in the result buffer as 16 unsigned shorts. The implementation is based on
the implementation found at [https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xorshift] (visited 10/6-2024).
With a good random number generator the 64 bits are random and independent and can
therefore be interpreted as 4 random unsigned shorts. One should keep in mind that pseudo
random number generators that do not rely on an external source of randomness aren’t
perfectly random, however this is sufficient for our needs.
To initialize these 8 64-bit unsigned integers (the seed of the random number generator)
we used std::chrono::high_resolution_clock::now().time_since_epoch().count() to seed the
built in random number generator with the srand() method and then populated the 8 64-bit
seeds with rand(). This does not need to be as statistically sound we just need these numbers
to be different between runs and for each node such that they dint generate the same random
numbers. This is also ensured as two nodes starting at exactly the same time is incredibly
unlikely.
Figure 7: Implementation for the random number generator
6.3
Fitness functions
In this section we will cover how the fitness functions were implemented.
As discussed in the previous section we designed the fitness functions to evaluate fitness
of a subset of samples to avoid memory copying and also present an interface.
Page 24 of 107
==PAGE:31==
Master’s Project
6.4
Topologies
Figure 8: Implementation for base fitness class and OneMax fitness class
As seen in figure [8] a fitness class takes in a DynamicArray of uint16_t the starting
index and the size of a sample (The problem size). As an example we see the implementation
for OneMax that sums over the section. Recall that these uint16_t is always 1 or 0;
MaxSat
For a more complex fitness function like MaxSat the instance is represented as
a vector of clauses which each are a pair of vectors containing the boolean variables that
comprises the clause and the list of these Boolean variables that are negated.
The fitness of the sample is then calculated by iterating over each clause and for each
Boolean variable in the clause we XOR the value of the boolean variable in the sample with
the negation sign which tells is if this clause on CNF form is satisfied. If so the sum is
incremented and finally the total number of satisfied clauses is returned as the fitness. (See
figure [9] for implementation)
Figure 9: Implementation for MaxSat fitness class
6.4
Topologies
For the topologies we needed to be able to query, for a given node in a cluster (one of the
MPI processes), which nodes to send to and which to receive from. We have implemented
a class Operation to represent such an operation, containing an op-code (Send or Receive)
and the MPI-Rank of the target node.
We have implemented the communication using
the blocking MPI methods, which means that topologies need to return an ordered list of
Page 25 of 107
==PAGE:32==
Master’s Project
6.4
Topologies
operations such that no deadlocks occur. For topologies we implemented a class for each
topology that takes in the MPI rank and size such that the output of the topology is an
ordered list of operations. The implementation for circle seen in figure 10 for example used
the ranks parity to know when to receive, send, and in what direction.
Figure 10: Implementation for Circle topology class
For the remaining topologies, similar implementation were made such that the list of
operations corresponds to the theoretical definition, and presented in the section Theoretical
Background.

==PAGE:33==
Master’s Project
6.5
Coordination
6.5
Coordination
Figure 11: Implementation for OptimizerCoordinator class
To coordinate the different node in a cluster (represented as an MPI process) we imple-
mented the OptimizerCoordinator class that takes the EDA, MigrationStrategy, Topology,
max amount of iterations and a stopping criteria.
The stopping criteria is a function that given an EDA instance returns a boolean indi-
cating whether to stop. This was typically evaluated on the best fitness seen so far, such
that when the optimal solution was found it should halt.
The method run calculates given the max iterations how many migrations can be done
at most and then in a loop we check if any of the nodes in the cluster signals that it should
halt, and if so it returns. Otherwise we run the amount of iterations specified in the migration
interval locally and apply the migration strategy. Any remaining iterations are run at the
end.
Page 27 of 107
==PAGE:34==
Master’s Project
6.5
Coordination
Figure 12: Implementation for communicating samples containers method
For communication we implemented a few methods using MPI to communicate the
common data structures we used for Sample and probability distribution representation. In
figure [12] we see the implementation for communicating SampleContainer. We first allocate
a buffer received_samples to contain all the samples we need to receive, where the amount
of samples to be received is known based on the amount of receive operations and the local
processes’ λ. Every operation has a switch case where we send our samples to the target rank,
or receive into our buffer from the target rank. Finally the received data is returned. For
the SampleContainer we communicate both the samples and the corrorsponding fitnesses.
The methods for communicating DynamicArray<t> the method was implemented similarly.
For the ShouldHalt method (used to query if any node in the cluster signals to halt/stop),
each node evaluates their StoppingCriteria and if true sends a message to all other nodes
asynchronously that they should halt. All nodes then continues to query with IProbe if
they have received any messages to halt (they might receive multiple) and finally each node
returns true if another node signalled to halt or if they evaluated their Stopping criteria to
true.
As covered in the section EDA each EDA captures a snapshot of some metrics every
iteration, and this is also written as the output for later data parsing. However there might
be a large amounts of iterations and due to storage limitations this needs to be truncated.
This truncation also needs to have a distributed implementation, as each node in a cluster
will likely have found the best fitness it has seen at different iterations. If we want to look
at all the nodes in a cluster at the first iteration where a node found the optimal solution,
then each node’s iteration at this point needs to be present in all the outputs from all nodes.
Considering this we implemented a Distributed_truncate method where each node sends the
iteration number for their best found solution, and each node then includes its snapshot at
this iteration in its output. This limits the amount of linear interpolation done in the data
Page 28 of 107
==PAGE:35==
Master’s Project
6.6
MigrationStrategy
parser, and for many of the visualizations eliminated it.
For writing these snapshots and the configuration and parameter setting of a node, we
implemented a simple JSON writer class that given these snapshots writes a JSON file.
6.6
MigrationStrategy
In this section we will cover the migration strategies and how they were implemented.
MigrationStrategy
The migration strategy was implemented with an abstract base class
that stores the migration interval and exposes a pure abstract method called do_migration(EDABase&
optimizer, Topology& topology). This method given an EDA and Topology calculated (using
the topology) the ordered set of operations and using the MPIWrapper class gets the set of
samples it should receive, and sends the local ones to other nodes. It then, using the ED-
ABase interface, applies the migration strategy. For the base class this is not implemented.
Figure 13: Implementation for SampleMigrationStrategy do_migration method
SampleMigrationStrategy
For the Sample migration strategy we see the implementa-
tion in figure [13]. This was implemented as follows: It gets the set of ordered operations,
and then communicates its own SampleContainer and gets the set of received samples. It
then concatenates its own with the received set and calls through the EDA interface up-
date_probability_distribution. This was implemented differently for each EDA as described
in 4.2.2 and 4.2.1.
The rest of the migration strategies were implemented similarly calling different methods
through the EDA interface.
6.7
EDA
This section will cover the implementation of the EDA’s and the base class.
EDABase
EDABase is an abstract class that contain the parameter settings for the EDA
such as the problem size (_n), λ (_lambda), µ (_mu) as well as the lower and upper bound
of the probability vector, iteration and fitness evaluation count, the PRNG and buffers for
Samples, the probability distribution and random numbers. Note the lower and upper bound
selected for all tests were 1
n and 1 −1
n respectively.
Page 29 of 107
==PAGE:36==
Master’s Project
6.7
EDA
The EDABase class exposes the interface used by the migration strategies implemented
as abstract methods. and itself has the implementation for Sampling and evaluating the
fitness as seen in figure [14]
Figure 14: Implementation for the sampling method
For performance reasons this method was also implemented using vectorization. The
method first gets the needed amount of newly generated pseudo random numbers and stores
them in the buffer. Using vector instructions it then compares 16 numbers to the probability
vector at a time and stores the result in the sample container which constitutes 16 bits
of a sample.
Notably there is not epi16 instructions for unsigned shorts so to have the
unsigned comparison the sign bit was flipped and dues to the integer representation of
negative numbers this yields the correct result. The result did however not return 1 and 0
so the least significant bit was taken which is 1 or 0 depending on the comparison.
By doing 16 comparisons at once requires that the remaining bits are handled separately
as the problem size might not be divisible by 16.
Lastly the method evaluates the fitness of each of these samples and increments the
fitness evaluations counter.
The EDABase class also contain the method for logging at each iteration, here we add
an instance of Iteration_Snapshot containing metrics to a vector. The metrics logged are
Iteration count, fitness evaluation count, lower and upper bound hits in the probability
distribution, best, worst and median fitness for the set of samples this iteration. It also
keeps track of the best fitness seen so far and at which iteration.
6.7.1
UMDA
The UMDA class contains the parameters not present in EDABase which are relevant
for UMDA. The algorithm was implemented as described in the theory section. In figure [15]
Page 30 of 107
==PAGE:37==
Master’s Project
6.7
EDA
we see the implementation of the run method. This was used to run the algorithm with an
iteration limit and a stopping criteria. The method first samples from the probability vector
and does an index sort of the samples (this avoids copying of large samples in memory). It
then update the probability vector and logs the state. Lastly it check if the stopping criteria
is met and increments the iteration counter.
Figure 15: Implementation for the run method for UMDA
The UMDA class additionally exposes some methods used by the migration strategies
umda::update_probability_distribution
This method was used for both the running
of the UMDA algorithm and the Sample migration strategy.
This was implemented by
summing over each bit of the µ best samples, and for each probability in the probability
distribution we set the new probability to the sum over µ enforcing the lower and upper
bound of the probability vector. The calculations were done using doubles and then scaled
to the uint16_t and rounded. (see figure [187] for implementation screenshot).
umda::force_update_probability_distribution
This method was used for the MuSam-
ple migration strategy where we do not sort and select the µ best samples but use all provided
samples. The implementation of this method was identical to the umda::update_probability_distribution
with the afformentioned changes.
umda::combine_probability_distributions
This method was used by the probability
migration strategy to do a weighted average of the incoming probability distributions and
the local one. The implementation is as follows: For each probability in each of the incoming
probability distributions the sum is calculated and then averaged and scaled with 1−β. The
local probability distribution is then scaled with β and the sum of the probability distribu-
tions (for each probability) is then rounded and stored as the new probability distribution.
For implementation screenshot see figure [188]
6.7.2
cGA
Similar to UMDA tha class CGA is the implementation of the cGA algorithm with an ad-
ditional parameter for population size. The run_iteration method is implemented similarly
for cGa as for UMDA. For cGA there are also implementations for updating the probability
distribution both used by the run_iterations method and the migration strategies.
Page 31 of 107
==PAGE:38==
Master’s Project
6.7
EDA
CGA::_update_probability_distribution
This method was used by both the run_iterations
method for cGA and the Sample migration strategy. This method was implemented based
on the description in the theory section. It first finds the best of the two samples, and the
worst (for implementation screenshot see figure [16]).
When updating the probability distribution for cGA we calculate the step based the
difference in bit settings for each bit index in the samples divided by the population size
scaled with the size of uint16_t. This difference is then added to the existing probability at
that bit index while enforcing the lower an upper bounds. Note these calculations were done
using Double and rounded before being stored. Note when this method is called from the
run_iterations method the parameter weight is always 1. This was implemented as described
in the theory section. For implementation screenshot see figure [17]
Figure 16: Implementation for the update probability distribution with best method for cGA
Figure 17: Implementation for the update probability distribution method for cGA
CGA::_update_probability_distribution_with_x_best
This method was used by
the MuSample migration strategy for cGA. it is implemented as described in the theory sec-
tion. Note the migration strategy always sets the parameter x (the amount of samples to be
used for the update is always larger than the sample size).
The implementation is as follows: The set of samples is a set of sample pair (2 from
each node) and for each pair the best and worst sample, by fitness, is identified. The for
each par the step (change in probability distribution) is calculated and divided by the the
population size (K) and the amount of steps (amount of pairs). This results in an offset
vector that for each probability has the average of the change to that probability amongst
all pairs. This is then used to update the local probability distribution while enforcing the
lower and upper bound. Note all calculations were done using Double and rounded at the
end. For implementation screenshot see figure [18]
Page 32 of 107
==PAGE:39==
Master’s Project

CGA::combine_probability_distributions
This method was implemented identically
to the version for UMDA. See 6.7.1 for describtion .
6.8
Testing
The tests were written in C++ (examples can be found in the Experiments directory in
the Algorithms project). The tests were set up in methods where for each configuration to be
tested, a migration strategy, topology, and EDA was provided to the OptimizerCoordinator
and run. This output was typically truncated by the DataTruncator class and written using
the JsonWriter.
Supporting classes to generate filenames based on the configurations and create a list of
the configuration and parameter settings for the output files, was implemented for the easy
of automation.
6.9
DataParsing
The data parsing was implemented in C#, where we use the libraries LINQ (lan-
guage integrated query) and TPL (task parallel library) for grouping and parallelization.
A DataReader class was implemented that reads the data JSON files and provides an in
memory representation. A DataSelector class was also implemented to aggregate, group
and select data from this in memory representation of our test data. These classes were
Page 33 of 107
==PAGE:40==
Master’s Project
use in the main class Parser where the data was read grouped and ordered using these sup-
porting classes. To generate the tables and graphs Maple and Latex support classes were
implemented that provide data structures to represent Maple matrices, and Latex Tables.
These classes also provide methods for generating textual representations that can be vi-
sualized in both programs. To do the statistical calculations for the Latex tables, a class
MathHelpers was implented that exposes method to calculate basic statistical metric such
as, mean, median, percentiles, and standard deviation.
To provide an easy way of naming these tables, and graphs a class DisplayNameFormat-
ter was implemented that, given a description and configuration could provide a readable
name.
This was used to name graphs and tables according to the algorithm, topology,
migration strategy and parameter configuration.
Grouping and ordering data was done with the help of the class Configuration. this
class allowed for group on the parameter setting, run, MPI rank, and other configuration
and parameter settings settings used for the experiments.
To perform more complex statistical calculations, the data parsing also included an
implementations of a code generator. This method allowed us to generate R code, which
could do the statistical calculations such as χ2, Fisher test, and Man-Whitney and provide
textual outputs representing Latex tables. This was implemented using text templates.
7
Experiments
In this section evaluates the cGA and UMDA algorithm with a combination of topolo-
gies (Complete, Circle, Star, and Torus) and migration strategies (Sample, MuSample, and
probability migration). A combination of an EDA, topology, and migration strategy will be
referred to as a configuration. In the next sections this thesis will cover an evaluation of
how each of these configurations behave on multiple problems (OneMax, LeadingOnes, Two-
Peaks, JumpK, and MaxSat) with changing migration interval (MI), how they scale w.r.t.
the problem size, and how they scale when the cluster size is increased.
The sections for each of the problems is structured as follows: We first find a suitable
population size (µ and λ) for UMDA and K for cGA for a fixed problem size. We then run
tests for each of these configurations for varying migration intervals and discuss the findings.
For comparison we also run NoMigration which is a configuration where the nodes don’t
communicate but all nodes stop when one finds the optimal solution. We then select the
best found migration intervals and see how the algorithm, with this fixed migration interval
and population size, scale with the problem size. Lastly we see if increasing the cluster
size affects performance. Note, there is no guarantee that fixing the populations size and
migration interval independently of the problem- and cluster size will yield a good parameter
selection. This however was used as a rule of thumb as otherwise a larger search space of
parameters would have to be considered.
In the following sections we will use abbreviations for some of the concepts, especially
in graphs and visualizations for space purposes. Migration interval is often MI, Probability
migration strategy might be referred to as Prob, Circle topology is sometimes referred to as
the as the more general type KCircle, but Circle is meant.
Page 34 of 107
==PAGE:41==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
The tests where carried out with cluster size 16 unless specified otherwise.
16 was
chosen due to its relatively large size and the fact its square number as well as a power of
two, making us able to test Torus on it.
A brief introduction of how data was handled for this section
For each test with
some configuration and parameter setting we ran a cluster of size 1 for the serial tests and
cluster of size 16 for the rest unless specified otherwise. For each node in the cluster we take
snapshots in memory of the state of the algorithm at that iteration. This information includes
Best, Median, and Worst fitness seen in this iteration, the iteration number, Lower and Upper
bound hits in the probability vector, the best fitness seen so far and at which iteration. Each
of these nodes store this as a Json file along with the configuration, parameters and MPI
information such that we can uniquely identify them and which run and experiment they
were part of. The parsing then consists of grouping and aggregating this data to produce
visualization in the form of plots and tables. We will evaluate using fitness evaluations.
These are chosen by selecting the first iteration on which the solution was found in the
cluster. At that iteration the node with the highest amount of fitness evaluation dictated
the metric we used in our analysis. This can in terms of fitness evaluations be considered
the parallel runtime. Note that the serial runtime by this definition is just the parallel scaled
with cluster size.
We use the appendix for graphs that help investigate the behavior and explain the graphs
seen in the report. The appendix also contain the statistical test which will be referred when
needed.

OneMax
This section dives into our findings when running
