7.1.1.1
Choice of µ and λ
For µ and λ we chose to go with a constant ratio
of 3e, as chosen in [43], while varying λ. We tested
number values in the range of 1 to 450 in increments of
1, running each experiment 4096 times . The results
are described in this section.
Looking at figure 19 the bimodality of OneMax
[20] can be observed as well as what could be inter-
Page 35 of 107
==PAGE:42==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
preted as noise, which seems more prominent at lower
values of λ. The noise comes from the ratio between µ and λ changing with the progression
of λ since only every 3e λ values does µ increase. The values 367 and 17 were chosen as
the two local minima. They are used for all future experiments on OneMax. These two
local minima will be referred to by the theoretical scaling of the population size as the (lg
n)-paradigm and (√n lg n)-paradigm.
7.1.1.2
Parallel Runs
In the next sections the performance of various topologies and migration strategies will
be investigated. To get an acurate comparison of behaviors as well as improvements a test
is performed. The test is done by running 16 nodes in parallel and stopping all of them once
the solution has been found by at least one of the nodes. The test is run 256 times.
Using (lg n)-paradigm the average amount of fitness evaluations was found to be 12,439.29
and using the (√n lg n)-paradigm it was found to be 11,619.28. See figure 8
7.1.1.3
Migration interval
For the choice of migration values we chose to run the algorithm with values of 1 to
1000 for the (lg n)-paradigm and 1 to 50 for the (√n lg n)-paradigm. These intervals were
chosen as they just exceed the average amount of iterations the algorithm uses to finish. For
each of them 20 points where made, evenly distributed including 1. Each experiment was
run 256 times.
Complete
In figure 20 a pattern emerges for the log(n) paradigm where certain intervals
of MI-values (migration interval values) deviate from the general curve.
Figure 20: A plot of how MI affects the amount of fitness evaluations it takes to find the
maximum fitness using UMDA and the Complete topology on OneMax. On the left the
log(n) paradigm is used, on the right
p
(n)log(n) paradigm
To investigate the deviation, certain points along the curve were selected. Specifically
MI=[1, 211, 369, 579, 842] were selected. Investigating how UMDA using Complete topology
and Sample migration progresses over time using these MI-values we get the following graph
Page 36 of 107
==PAGE:43==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
(see figure 21):
Figure 21: A graph of how the log(n) paradigm progresses over time with certain MI values
using the Complete topology and Sample migration strategy
On the graph above we can see that at the points of migration the fitness is greatly
increased, as the cluster of nodes progress closer to finding the optimal solution the impact
on the fitness as a result of migration is decreased. This is due to the algorithm progressing
slower the closer to the optimal solution it gets, which gives less progress to share.
This suggests a benefit to migrating farther away from the optimal solution. This would
result in the migration interval being optimal when it is dividable with the iterations it takes
the algorithm to finish in such a way that the remainder is maximised. As migration interval
increases this remainder decreases until one less migration is done, but the remainder is
maximised again. Note more frequent migration still out weighs this effect.
Given the similar behaviour of the two other migration strategies as seen in figure 20,
we expect the same phenomenon is what affects MuSample and Prob for the
p
(n)log(n)
paradigm.
A migration interval of 1 was found to be optimal for all three migration strategies using
the complete topology. In the case of OneMax this is expected as there are no downsides to
sharing information [9].
The general behavior observed from figure 20 was an increase in fitness evaluations as MI
increases and approaches NoMigration. Once MI has increased to about 800 the performance
equals NoMigration and no further change in fitness evaluations can be observed as MI
increases.
Torus
In figure 88 a similar pattern to Complete can be observed for Torus,
Circle
Like for Torus, figure 89 reveals that Circle gives similar results as Complete
Star
In figure 90 it can be observed that the benefits are a lot smaller than using eg.
Complete.
The important point is that Star enforces diversity in the island model through lack of
communication towards the outer nodes. The center node is the only one that can be affected
and as such only it can be positively or negatively impacted by migration. From this it can
Page 37 of 107
==PAGE:44==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
be noted that the benefits of the center node is removed with too frequent migration as it
gets constantly overwritten by the outer nodes. As migration interval increases the effect of
overwriting the middle node is lessened and the effect of less benefits from migration takes
over.
Looking at the (√nlog(n))-paradigm the effect can be observed in depth.
Focusing on Musample the effect of overwriting the center node has negative impact
on the amount of fitness evaluations spent compared to NoMigration. This is caused by
the center node being overwritten with the average of all nodes, keeping it from being an
active participant in progress of the cluster. As MI increases the center node gets to progress
independently and after about MI = 5 the effect of being set to the average fitness helps it
progress as seen in Complete topology. After about MI = 15 the positive effect of migration
is halted by the fewer amount of migrations, an effect that can also be observed for the
Complete topology.
Both Probability migration and Sample migration are also affected by this overwriting
effect but to a lesser degree. For Probability migration this is due to its 90% beta value,
making sure it only partially overwritten. It again meets a point were the effect of fewer
migration is larger than the effect of more independent progress. For Sample the effect is
limited due to it being set to the highest fitness currently in the cluster. This makes its
progress in those few iterations matter as it can surpass the rest of the cluster and be a
benefit. In some cases it needs more independent work to do so and as such it benefits from
a higher MI value until the effect of fewer migrations outweigh the effect of independent
work.
Connectivity
While the three topologies of Complete, Torus and Circle have similar
graphs, they follow a pattern where Complete gives slightly better results than Torus, which
in turn gives slightly better results than Circle. This is expected as, there’s no downside to
sharing information [9].
Improvements
Going with the largest improvements w.r.t. fitness evaluations for both
paradigms, the best migration strategies were MuSample for (lg n)-paradigm and Sample
for (√n lg n)-paradigm, both running on the Complete topology.
Complete-Sample was chosen for the (√n lg n)-paradigm due to a significant difference
in numerical values9.
MuSample was chosen over Prob for the (lg n)-paradigm due to its slight numerical
improvement9 together with a Mann-Whitney U-Test (0.01009083) 10.
Respectivly for the (lg n)-paradigm and the (√n lg n)-paradigm we got 1053.34 and
8423.8 fitness evaluations.
In the serial test we got 14,161.22 and 16,162.81 as the average amount of fitness eval-
uations for the two paradigms. For the parallel runs we got 12,439.29 and 11,619.28. Now
for the best strategies and topologies we got, the results were 1,053.34 and 8,423.80.
Comparing to the serial results we get 1, 053.34/14, 161.22 = 0, 07438 for the log(n)
paradigm and 8, 423.80/16, 162.81 = 0, 52118 for

can do without using the island model, using the same hardware is about 11 times slower
than what weve achieved using the island model. This is of course a huge improvement, but
we must remember the simplicity of the problem in question. For more complex problems
we expect that migration does not improve the results as much due to it not always being
just an upside.
Chosen Mi Values
For most of the island models we found a migration interval of 1 to
be optimal. Only using the Star topology showed otherwise. Here values of 3, 16 and 3 were
chosen for Sample, MuSample and Probability migration using the sqrt(n)log(n) paradigm
and 53 was chosen for MuSample migration using the log(n) paradigm.

Connectivity
For the three topologies we have gone over thus far, the results match ex-
pectations pretty well. One point is worth noting though which is how the Sample migration
strategies scales depending on connectivity.
Figure 24: Cluster Tests for UMDA OneMax using the Sample migration strategy with
lambda=17 on the left and lambda=367 on the right
In figure 24, specifically the left side using the log(n) paradigm we see that Torus is the
most efficient. We’d expect an order of Complete > Torus > Circle where Complete is best
due to its large connectivity and the simple nature of OneMax, but there seems to exist some
local optima in connectivity using the smaller population size. The idea is that since there is
a smaller population size, one could benefit from diversity among the nodes. This diversity
can only exist with lower connectivity since we use such small migration intervals, thus a
lower connectivity is beneficial. At the same time a lower connectivity means slower rate
of information sharing which means the node furthest ahead’s information can have a hard
time reaching the one furthest behind. Thus some balance in the connectivity is optimal
and Torus happens to be closer to that balance.
Star
For Star (see figure 96) it can be observed that for the log(n) paradigm, MuSample
and Probability migration both quickly flatten out. After a certain point averaging out so
many other nodes on one node doesnt make a difference. For the sqrt(n)log(n) paradigm it
should be noted that MuSample migration seems to do worse than NoMigration in the long
run.

Using the values λ = 15, µ = 1 we ran tests varying the Migration interval for different
combinations of topology and migration strategy. Mi was varied from 1 to 600 with 20 evenly
distributed point including 1 and 20. The tests were run 256 times.
Complete
Using the complete topology we expect little diversity and quick progress, which
are points that should help solving LeadingOnes.
Page 42 of 107
==PAGE:49==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
Figure 26: A plot of how mi affects the amount of fitness evaluations it takes to find the
maximum fitness using UMDA and the Complete topology on LeadingOnes
In figure 26 we can see that Sample does the best with higher migration intervals and
interestingly MuSample does worse than with NoMigration going up to about 6500 fitness
evaluations. This is rather interesting and to try and get an understanding we tried seeing
how genetic drift might influence the problem.
Figure 27: Two plots of how many extreme min and max values there are in the probability
vector.
In figure 27 we see how Sample gets massive boosts each time migration is performed
and how genetic drift exists in a very peculiar case for UMDA with µ = 1. We can see that at
all iterations the amount of lower and upper bound hits sum up to 100, which is all the bits.
This is because UMDA does not work on previous probability vectors, but rather makes a
new one each time and with µ = 1, this vector consists of solely max and min values. With
this knowledge we can conclude that genetic drift is not the difference between the migration
Page 43 of 107
==PAGE:50==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
strategies. We can tho draw a parallel to (µ + λ)EA since all of our points now always have
a 1/n probability of being flipped. With µ = 1, UMDA equals that of (1 + λ)EA.
Figure 28: Plots of how different migration strategies with different migration intervals
progress in fitness over evaluations.
Now looking at figure 28 we can see that Sample significantly benefits from migrations.
We also see that probability migration only has a noticeable benefit at very small migration
intervals, and lastly we see that MuSample decreases in fitness on migration, at least for
higher intervals.
Our intuitive understanding of why this happens is that the samples shared have a
significant risk of sampling a 0 at a lower index than the current best fitness. The chance of
this happening is bound to the probability of previous bits. As some have hit upper bound
the chance of them to be flipped is low, but recent significant bits would still have a high risk
of being flipped. This effect outweighs the chance of sampling higher which comes from, on
average 50% per bit. Migrating these lower fitness sample would then negativly impact all
nodes. One might expect similar effects for Probability migration, but with it not sharing
samples (instead sharing current progress) and with β determining how affected each note
is, the effect is severely dampened.
Torus
Torus shows similar behavior to Complete. The results can be seen on figure 97.
It should also be noted here that Sample migration seems negatively impacted by the
switch from Complete to Torus topology. We presume this has to do with connectivity, and
if this holds true we should be able to observe a similar behavior.
Circle
For the Circle topology the trends that can be seen in figure 98 seem similar to those
of the Complete topology as well as the Torus topology. Observe also here that the fitness
evaluations of the Sample migration strategy tend to be higher than that of the Complete
and Torus topology for higher migration intervals. A deeper dive into this phenomenon will
come in the next section.
Connectivity
For both MuSample and Probability migration there is so little difference
that we cannot say anything about how it scales. In figure 29 we can see the difference for
Page 44 of 107
==PAGE:51==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
Sample though.
Figure 29: A plot of how mi affects the amount of fitness evaluations it takes to find the
maximum fitness using UMDA and the Sample migration strategy on LeadingOnes
Here we see how the connectivity affects how well sample does. It can be seen that
Complete as stated before does best with higher migration intervals, but now it is also more
clear that it does better with mi = 1 as well. As Sample migration works a little like having
16 times as big a λ (16 nodes) without the drawback of extra fitness evaluations, this result
was expected.
Star
For Star, figre 99, we can make the argument that Sample migration does better with
lower migration interval, which would give the middle node a better chance of reaching the
end point faster. The excact decrease in fitness evaluations though is only about (6188.61 −
5800)/6188.61 = 0.06279. At just over 6 percent it is noticable, but not that impressive.
We also speculate that MuSample and Probability migration has little to no effect when
using star topology. This is probably due to the middle node just becomes the average of all
other nodes.
Improvements
In the end the best combination of migration strategy and topology we
found was Sample migration on the Complete topology. On the results table 25 one can
see that it on average used 1.299.96 fitness evaluations. We know from table 24 that using
NoMigration we got 6188.61 fitness evaluations. Comparing these two we can say that the
migration strategy and topology used (1299.96/6188.61 = 0, 21006) about 21% of the fitness
evaluations.

We know the ratio set to be 3e = 8.15, so at n = 60 the change should begin. At n = 50
the change should be noticable, which it is on the graph. Further at n = 10 and n = 20
our results should resemble that of (1, 1)EA, which it seems to do. This is why we get not
a spike going up as n becomes smaller but a hump, as we enter a new way of solving the
problem.
To make sure our understanding was correct, we ran the population size test, allowing
both µ and λ to move freely this time and then ran a population size test where λ was bound
to µ instead of the other way around. We got λ = 13 and µ = 1 and then the following
graph.

7.1.3
TwoPeaks
This section covers our tests for UMDA on the TwoPeaks problem. We generally expect
similar results to LeadingOnes when running parallel and serial. Conflict between the nodes
Page 49 of 107
==PAGE:56==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
is expected when doing migration due to different nodes optimizing different problems. As
it resembles LeadingOnes the chosen problem size was 100.
7.1.3.1
Choice of µ and λ
To pick µ and λ a ratio of 3e was held as λ was varied from 1 to 450 at increments of
1. Each experiment was run 256 times and the resulting graph can be seen in figure 36.
Figure 36: A plot of how many fitness evaluations it took for UMDA to solve the TwoPeaks
problem when varying lambda
A very similar result to LeadingOnes is observed as expected. To get a better comparison
see figure 37.
Figure 37: A plot of how many fitness evaluations it took for UMDA to solve the TwoPeaks
and LeadingOnes problems when varying lambda
In figure 37 two things can be seen. Firstly the lowest amount of fitness evaluations
TwoPeaks uses is less fitness evaluations evaluations than LeadingOnes. Secondly TwoPeaks
does worse than LeadingOnes in terms of fitness evaluations for higher λ values. The reason
it uses less fitness evaluations than LeadingOnes for lower values of λ is that it can now
solve the problem from both ends. This benefits the starting point (the first samples) of the
algorithms as the chance of starting with a fitness x is increased by having both LeadingOnes
and TrailingZeroes to evaluate on.
Page 50 of 107
==PAGE:57==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
Having two fitness functions to optimize towards is also the reason it scales worse. With
a µ of 1, it can only ever be working on one of the two fitness functions (While it is possible
for it to alternate between the two fitness functions, it is very improbable). In this sense
only the chances of good starting samples have an effect. With a large enough population
it can start working on both fitness functions as in one generation there might be selected
solutions optimizing both ends. It can even be preferable to only working on one as, if one
end were to flip a "solved" bit of one end the other can work as a backup fitness. This
means that when the two problems meet, (normally at the center), UMDA has to figure out
which of the two to optimize towards and then has to completely flip the probabilities of the
remaining bits. One can see it as forced extreme genetic drift. In conclusion we end with a
λ of 16 and µ of 1.
7.1.3.2
Parallel Runs
For the sake of having something to compare to, a test where 16 nodes were run indi-
vidually and all stopped when one finished was run. Running this 256 times gives us an idea
of how our island model compares to using the same computing power to find the solution
without any island model. In table 33 we can see the algorithm uses on average 6,129.43
fitness evaluations running this way.
7.1.3.3
Migration interval
For the choice of migration values we chose to run the algorithm with values of 1 to
600 choosing 20 evenly spread points including 1 and 600. For probability migration more
points between 1 and 100 where chosen to better show its behavior. Each experiment was
run 256 times.
Complete
In figure 38 many observations can be made.
The first observations is that Sample kills diversity and through this manages to not
fight itself.
The second observation is that MuSample uses more fitness evaluations the higher the
migration interval, until a point just before Mi = 500 where it starts decreasing.
The
intuitive explanation for this is that in between migrations more progress is made which
means on migration there is more overlap in bits worked on by two opposing nodes.
The last observation is with Probability migration. With Mi = 1 it barely does worse
than not migrating at all leading us to believe the nodes still dont always align on what
problem to solve. Then as Mi is increased the chance of alignment rapidly decreases until
about Mi = 50 where the effects of misalignment are lessened by the lessened frequency.
We expect the reason why the effects of mis-alignment so quickly subside are the same as
why the chances of misalignment are so high. The β variable of the probability migration
strategy makes it difficult for nodes to align as they are less affected by others than eg. using
MuSample migration. Since they are less affected though the setbacks of misalignment are
also dampened by β.
Page 51 of 107
==PAGE:58==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
Figure 38: A plot of how mi affects the amount of fitness evaluations it takes to find the
maximum fitness using UMDA and the Complete topology on TwoPeaks
All of the above merely scratches the surface though and well go deeper into all of it
later in this section.
Torus
Looking at figure 106 and comparing it to figure 38 a few things can be seen. The
max point for MuSample has moved left and gone up in fitness evaluations. Probability
migration’s spike has decreased in width and lessened in height.
The findings mentioned above will be delved into later in this section.
Circle
For circle (figure 107) the pattern for Probability migration changes as the spike
has decreased significantly in height. The MuSample migration shows that there was a local
optimum for Fitness Evaluations at Torus.
Migration Strategies
Having gone over the three main topologies, we now go more in
depth on each migration strategies, as they show peculiar patterns. For Sample
Sample
Sample migration’s graphs show us the benefits of discouraging diversity.
All
nodes connected to the one furthest ahead in fitness all align on migration. With a higher
µ certain issues might occur where selected samples are optimised towards different fitness
functions. With µ = 1 only the sample with the highest fitness influences the probability
vector.
MuSample and Probability migration
To test why MuSample and Probability both
did worse, why they looked different and why mi = 1 might have been a good option we
plotted how fitness progressed over fitness evaluations. See figure 39 for the results.
Page 52 of 107
==PAGE:59==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
Figure 39: Plots of how different migration intervals progress in fitness over time. Complete
topology(left and right). MuSample(left). Prob(right).
We can note a few things. Probability migration has much smaller drops than MuSample
migration. This is simply explained by the β variable for Probability, causing migration to
only have a 10% effect. We also notice that Probability migration seems to continue for much
longer than MuSample. For this we would like to point out the size of the drops as time
goes on. They get smaller and small the longer the algorithm has run, but seem to do more
so for MuSample than Probability migration. We presume two reasons for this decrease in
drop size. One is our parser that averages out over the fitness axis. Thereby after the point
of solving for one run it will have no more drops, and averaging out with other runs after
this point will make their drops smaller visually. The other factor is aligning on the problem
theyre solving. The reason why mi = 1 on MuSample does so well is that it is quick to make
all nodes align on solving either LeadingOnes or TrailingZeroes. For Probability migration
only 10% effect is taken, making it way harder to align.
This explanation for dropsize explains why Probability migration takes so long for lower
mi values. Alignment becomes very difficult, when only 10% effect is taken, and thus dropsize
wont decrease.
On the other hand for larger mi values, only taking 10% migration effect, means that
they dont lose as much, and they can actually progress more than they lose on migration.
This alignment is also why MuSample does worse on Torus topology, than on Complete.
We assume the more connectivity, the bigger the setbacks as theres a larger chance of it. At
the same time the more cennectivity, the higher chance of aligning nodes on what problem
to solve.
Figure 40: A plot of how different topologies using MuSample progress over time on Two-
Peaks.
Further supporting this theory, looking at how mi = 50 progresses over time (see figure
40) it can be observed that on the first migration, Complete is set back the furthest and
Page 53 of 107
==PAGE:60==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
Circle is set back the least. We then see that Complete gets ahead, by its losses in fitness
becoming smaller than the other two topologies. Meanwhile, Circle does better than Torus
by simply having smaller setbacks from the beginning. In this sense Torus is near a local
minimum for performance as it has bigger initial setbacks than circle and bigger setbacks
than Complete nearing the end.
Star
For Star the effect of migration is very small, but some general trends can be seen.
Sample does generally better as it allows the middle node to be up to date with the node
furthest ahead at all times. For MuSample and Probability migration we can use the fact
that alignment cant happen as the nodes do not communicate back and forth. Therefore,
any migration is negative, but only for the middle node.
Figure 41: A plot of how mi affects the amount of fitness evaluations it takes to find the
maximum fitness using UMDA and the Star topology on TwoPeaks
Choice of migration interval
For all combinations of topology and migration strategy
weve chosen a migration interval of 1 to use for future tests on TwoPeaks. While we recognise
mi = 1 isnt always the best, we chose to use mi = 1 for all combinations of topology and
migration strategy since for those where it wasnt the best, the best thing to do was to not
migrate at all.

7.1.5
MaxSat
To evaluate UMDA on the MaxSat problem we have used the source of instances as
described in the Theory section. Each of these problem sizes contain many instances in CNF
form and we haven chosen to run the population size test on the first 6 instances from each
problems size, we then select 3 instances of appropriate difficulty.
7.1.5.1
Population Size
For MaxSat we tested µ and λ values by setting µ to log(n) multiplied by some variable
(cm) we scaled from 1 to 5 and setting to µ times some variable (ck) we scaled from 2 to 5
for a total of 20 combinations. ck controls the ratio between µ and λ so the higher it is the
more elitist the algorithm is. cm and cl together control the general size of the population
generated, making each iteration more exploratory. We test these 20 combinations for the
first 6 instances for problem sizes 25-200 in increments of 25 to find suitable instances to
perform experiments with. For UMDA we found that a good level of complexity was 200
boolean variables with 860 clauses, and selected instance 1, 3, and 5 as they provide a simple

7.1.5.2
NoMigration
For the purpose of having something to compare our island models to, we run the 3
instances with cluster size 16, without any migration. The idea is to show how well the
algorithm does if its allowed the same computational power as the island models, without
the actual models.
What we note is the high standard deviation of the fitness evaluations the lowest com-
pared to the mean being Instance 1 with a standard deviation equal to about 80% of the
mean. This high deviation comes from the difficulty of the problem, even when using multiple
nodes.
7.1.5.3
Migration interval
For the choice of migration interval we chose to run the algorithm with mi values of 2j
varying j from 0 to 19, getting mi values from 1 to 262144. With average iterations going
up to 1 million (see instance 1 and 5 75th percentile, figure 4), going up to 262 thousand
seemed appropriate as higher values would only get closer to NoMigration. Each experiment
was run 50 to 51 times.
The plots we created differed for instance 1 and 5 compared to 3. For 3 we focused
on the amount of fitness evaluations it took the problem, while for 1 and 5 the success-rate
was plotted as the y-axis, showing how the different topologies can increase or decrease the
success.
Complete
.
For complete, instance 1 and 5 show similar patterns for the behavior of the different
migration strategies (see figure 49).
We see that the effect of probability migration is very negative with small mi values,
but quickly the negative effects subside. We suspect this to be a result of how much progress
can be made between migrations and the size of β for Probability migration. We suspect
Page 62 of 107
==PAGE:69==
Master’s Project
7.1
UMDA
that the point where the negative effects have subsided is where the algorithm has time to
do progress between migrations.
A small phenomena exists where it will spike just as the negative effects subside and
while it might be a result of deviation, the phenomena also exists for Torus and Complete
topology, see figures 122 and 124. To test the significance of this phenomenon we would
test more mi-values near these spikes and increase the amount of runs, but due to time-
constraints of our project we have not been able to do so. Our intuition on why this might
exist is that at this point the probability migration strategy works as a way to re-introduce
diversity in each algorithm, allowing a broader search than without it.
For Sample and MuSample we see equally negative effects, but they subside over a much
larger interval of migration intervals. For sample, the killing of diversity is expected to give
lower success rates, as the complexity of the problem requires high exploration. MuSamples
results are more interesting. It differs largely from Probability migration, which it normally
closely relates to and instead follows a curve more akin to Sample. We suspect this to come
from the complexity of the problem where even 1 bit flipped can make a huge difference in
fitness making sharing samples very chaotic.
On Complete as well as Torus and Circle (see figures 122 and 124) MuSample also
seems to be growing beyond NoMigrations successrate as migration interval goes towards
262 thousand. We cannot confirm the significance of this phenomenon, but given more time
we would test the surrounding Mi-values and increase the amount of runs to confirm or deny
its existence. If it does exist, it would seem that very rarely introducing diversity through
samples can help the algorithm when it gets stuck.

7.2.1
OneMax
In this section we investigate the properties of cGA on OneMax with different migration
strategies and topologies with varying population size, migration intervals, problem sizes and
cluster sizes.
7.2.1.1
Population size Test
To test the population size we conducted experiments for different populations sizes in
the range 1-400 in increments of 1. This was averaged over 4096 runs for problem size 1000.
This section describes the results.
In figure 126 it can be observed that cGA shows bi-modality for two local minima of
the value K (population size) as supported by theory[23] at around K = 11 and K = 132.
Where the optimization time is respectively 17.790,62 and 14.620,73 fitness evaluations
43. These values for K will be used for the experiments for cGA on OneMax.
7.2.1.2
Migration interval Test
To investigate the properties of cGA with varying migration intervals (MI) on OneMax
each test will be run for N = 1000 with the two values for K (Population size) as found
in the previous section and cluster size 16. The migration intervals that were tested are
⌊1.5x⌋, x ∈[0; 20] and averaged over 256 runs. This section describes the results.
NoMigration
First considering NoMigration where we do not allow any migration and
once one of the 16 nodes has found a solution, all nodes stop. This was averaged over 256
runs. We see that for k = 11 and 132 the average amount of fitness evaluations required
has decreased to 13.703,31 and 12.111,64 respectively (see table44). This will serve as a
benchmark for comparison with the different migration strategies and topologies.
Complete topology
From figure 53 for K = 11it can be observed that the Sample strategy
benefits from a more greedy approach with frequent migration.
With higher migration
intervals it approaches the same performance as achieved with NoMigration. This is expected
as greedy approaches is generally beneficial on OneMax [9]. Similarly for K = 132 it benefits
from having a low migration interval, both found optimal migration intervals were 1 where
the largest amount of fitness evaluations were 10.630,92 for K = 11 (see table 46) and
10.366,91 for K = 132 (see table45), they thereby use approximately 0.78 and 0.86 of
the fitness evaluations of NoMigration respectively. Both the behavior in terms of varying
migration intervals and the performance with migration interval 1 does not seem to be highly
influenced by the population size as the two paradigms behave similarly and have similar
runtimes.
The strategies MuSample and Probability migration showed similar behavior to each
other where too small migration intervals would yield poor performance contrary to the
Sample migration strategy. For K = 11 we find the optimal migration intervals to be 11
Page 67 of 107
==PAGE:74==
Master’s Project
7.2
CGA
and 129 for MuSample and Probability migration respectively ( see table46). Both of these
strategies share a "diversity enforcing" approach which will to some extent average out all
solutions from the different nodes.
In this case if each node is not allowed to progress
and increase the probabilities of some bits in the probability vector, it seems that frequent
migration will prevent any node from progressing as any progress with be averaged out by the
other nodes. For MuSample, when each node updates during migration using information
from all other nodes one could imagine that they don’t all agree on which probabilities to
changes and might disagree where one node wishes to increase some probability and another
wishes to decrease the same probability. Genetic drift is also know to be a problem with
low values for K and genetic drift in one node might work counter productively against
progress from another node, especially for the probability migration strategy. However once
the migration interval is sufficiently high this does not seem to be an issue and increasing the
migration interval beyond the optimal provides less and less benefits and the performance
in terms of fitness evaluations approach that of NoMigration.
For K = 132 both MuSample and probability migration again does not benefit from
very small migration intervals. MuSample performed similarly to that of NoMigration with a
sufficiently high migration interval but did not seem to improve in terms of fitness evaluations.
Probability migration strategy showed some improvement for migration interval 25 (see
table45). needing 10.886,18 fitness evaluations, thereby improving upon NoMigration with
a factor of approxemately 0.9 (see table 45).
Interestingly the two best performing configurations were MuSample and Probability
migration for K = 11, where low migration intervals might perform poorly due to genetic
drift it seems that these migration stratgies with the right migration interval are able to
combat the effects of genetic drift as they outperform the same strategies but with K = 132
where genetic drift is less likely, or perhaps benefit sufficiently from the migration to the
point where genetic drift is less prominent in the results.
The best performing configuration on OneMax with cGA and complete topology was K
= 11 using probability migration and migration interval of 129 which yields an improvement
of approximately 0.41 (see table46).
For K = 11 in the presence of genetic drift, probability migration performs the best,
which indicates that it benefits from averaging the probabilities over all nodes, which in-
tuitively could minimize the genetic drift as all nodes are not like to all have genetic drift
of the same probabilities, however for K = 132 where genetic drift is less prominent, it is
outperformed by the Sample migration strategy.

KCircle topology
For the topology KCircle we observe very similar behavior amongst
the different strategies as compared to the Complete topology. For K = 132 the MuSample
migration strategy does not manage to improve significantly on NoMigration (see table 48)
and has a high optimal migration interval of 3325.
The Sample migration strategy still benefits most from a low migration interval of
1, however it does not benefit from the lower connectivity of KCircle compared to Com-
plete topology with average amount of fitness evaluations required being 11.797,59 and
11.318,02 (see table 47 and48) for K = 11 and 132 respectively. This intuitively could
be caused by a smaller set of samples to choose from each migration, and thereby the best
sample from is smaller set is likely not as good as the best one from a larger set. The lower
connectivity could however foster some diversity as the nodes does not receive the same set
of sample each at migration and would therefore not choose to update the probability vector
using the same one, however this does not seem to beneficial for a problem like OneMax
based on the results.
Similarly the MuSample migration strategy does not benefit the lower connectivity of
KCircle for K = 11 with the average amount of fitness evaluations required being 8.945,24
(see table 47) which is increased from 7.625,90 when using Complete topology (see table
46).
Considering their p-values for the Mann Whitney U-Test 4.1706910−7061 they are
significantly different. It does however have a lower optimal migration interval of 3 compared
to 11 as with the Complete topology. While a too low migration interval has the same effect
as with the Complete topology, the lower connectivity of KCircle allows for more frequent
migration before this affects the performance.
The lower connectivity of KCircle also allows for the probability migration strategy to
migrate more frequently with the optimal migration interval found being 57 for K = 11,
which gives an increase in performance where the required average max fitness evaluations
is 2.975,1 (see table 47) and a performance increase of a factor of approximately 0.22
Page 69 of 107
==PAGE:76==
Master’s Project
7.2
CGA
compared to NoMigration.
For K = 132 however there was no significant advantage to
having the lower connectivity of KCircle with the optimal migration interval being the same
as with the Complete topology of 25. This again could be explained by genetic drift not
causing performance loss when using probability migration strategy to the same extent and
the larger step size when the population size is 11 is beneficial for a problem like OneMax,
whereas with a population size of 132 the smaller step size limits its progression rate.

(b) K = 132

Star topology
For the star topology with population size 132 none of the strategies man-
aged to greatly improve on how many fitness evaluations finding the optimal solution took.
MuSample strategy needed a sufficiently high migration interval to perform similarly to that
of NoMigration, and with lower migration intervals the performance dropped. We expect
this behavior to be due to the average step taken of 15 independently working nodes to be
negative for the center node especially in the early iterations.
For Sample strategy a migration interval of 1 still provided the best results, yet the
improvement on NoMigration was insignificant in size, and for probability migration strategy,
similarly to previous topologies, a balanced migration interval did perform best. Both Sample
and Probability managed less than a 2 percent improvement in terms of fitness evaluations.
While the improvements are small they are significant with a p-value of 3.482072 · 10−8 for
Sample and 1.787162 · 10−10 for Probability migration (see table 51).
In addition the star topology as opposed to other topologies does not allow all nodes to
learn from one another, and the information from one node is not propagated to all nodes
eventually. This explains the small improvements, since only 1/16’th of the effect is possible.
For population size 11, Sample migration strategy did not seem to be affected by the
migration interval, performing similar to NoMigration. This is expected since the step of
the node most ahead should give as much of an improvement to the center node as its own
step. The only case where this does not hold true is when the fitness of the node furthest
Page 70 of 107
==PAGE:77==
Master’s Project
7.2
CGA
ahead is much greater than your own, in which case its steps provide less progress. In this
case the center node would be unlikely to be the one finding the optimal solution.
The MuSample strategy and probability migration strategy did manage to improve on
NoMigration with average max fitness evaluations being 11.113,06 and 10.232,98 respec-
tively. This suggests that accumulating the progress in one node is beneficial, provided that
the population size is small enough and in turn the step size is large enough for OneMax.
The reason behind this behavior is expected to be the same as for Complete, Torus and
KCircle which show similar behavior.
One could imagine that the step size/population size will depend on the problem where
OneMax generally benefits from smaller population size and larger step sizes whereas that
might not be the case for a different problem, this needs to be balanced with how much is
gained by sharing information as can be seen in this experiment where smaller step sizes
limits the benefit of migration.

Torus topology
For population size 132, Torus performs similar to both Complete and
KCircle topology for the migration strategies, with performance and connectivity of this

strategy seems to benefit from higher connectivity.
For Population size 11 both the Sample and MuSample migration strategy, on both
Complete and KCircle topology outperform Torus and does not benefit linearly from the
connectivity or how the information will propagate throughout the node with this topology.
For Propability migration it does however perform better than KCircle and worse than
Complete when using Torus topology.

7.2.1.3
Variable N Test
To investigate the properties of cGA with the different topologies and migration strate-
gies we run tests with the migration intervals and populations sizes found the two previous
sections with varying problem sizes ranging from 10-5000 in increments of 10. We let the
populations size scale with both log(N) · c and log(N)
p
(N) · c where c is a constant found
such that for N = 1000 the two functions equal 11 and 132 respectively thereby matching
the values for population size we found earlier. Each test will be run with cluster size 16
and averaged over 256 runs. This section describes the results.
Serial N Test
Running cGA for OneMax with increasing problem size we see in figure
[131] that for both population size 11 and 132 that it follows the theoretically expected
O(N · log(N)). This will be used for comparison in the following tests.
Complete
For population size 132 for Complete topology we see that both Sample and
MuSample migration strategy follows the theoretically expected O(N · log(N)) with some
constant factor of improvement. Probability migration seems to follow the same progression
until about N = 4000, and for population size 11 the same happens yet more drastically, as
the results indicate that both Probability migration and MuSample strategy has exponential
run-off’s at N = 2000 and N = 3000 respectively [57].
This was suspected to be caused by genetic drift as both Probability migration and
MuSample strategy will average out the probability vector of all nodes to some extent.
When N becomes sufficiently large with high probability all nodes will align their genetic
drift on some of the probabilities. Any increase in these aligned probabilities is averaged
out by migration. This forces a node to sample 1 at each of these bits being held at the
Page 72 of 107
==PAGE:79==
Master’s Project
7.2
CGA
lower bound ( 1
N ) all at once to find the optimal solution. With X probabilities held at this
lower bound we get a probability of finding the optimal solution on a given iteration equal to
(( 1
N )X). In figure 57c MuSample strategy seems to stagnate at increasingly higher amounts
of lower bound hits for increasing N, and thus if X is some function of N it would explain
the exponential runoff we see in figure[57]
For population size 11 Sample migration strategy also follow the theoretical progression
but improves by some constant factor.

KCircle

be caused by the lower connectivity making it less likely that all nodes experience genetic
drift to a lower bound in the same probability, as not all nodes receive probability vectors
from all other nodes as with Complete topology. This is also supported by how the shared
information propagates within this topology, as it would take N

7.2.1.4
Cluster size test
To test the scaling properties of cGA on OneMax with the different migration strategies
and topologies in terms of cluster size we run each migration strategy with each topology for
cluster sizes 2-32 in powers of 2 and for Torus we run with cluster sizes (quadratic numbers)
4, 9, 16, 25 for each of the populations sizes and migration intervals found in the previous
sections. For this experiment we use problem size 1000 and average over 256 runs. This
section describes the results.
Complete
For Complete topology and population size 11 (see figure [59a]) we see that
with NoMigration increasing the cluster size yields diminishing returns as expected, and
Page 74 of 107
==PAGE:81==
Master’s Project
7.2
CGA
similar behavior was observed for Sample migration strategy but with some constant factor
less fitness evaluations. For MuSample migration strategy there is a steeper slope in the
range of cluster sizes [2..16] where-after it has a similar gradient to that of NoMigration,
suggesting that it benefits more from increasing the cluster size and thereby the amount of
samples each node receives each migration up until cluster size 16. As MuSample for cGA
has all nodes contribute with a set of samples (constituting the step they want to take) and
have all nodes take the average of this (like a collective average step) it might benefit from
the diversity of more steps being included in this average which would explain the gradient
up until cluster size 16.
Counter intuitively we see that for Probability migration it actually performs worse with
increasing cluster size for population size 11. This suggests that it benefits more from giving
a higher weight to fewer probability vectors, than it does taking the average of many. This is
similar to what was observed in the migration interval tests between Complete and KCircle
topology, where the lower connectivity was beneficial.
For population size 132 (see figure [59b]), we see NoMigration has similar behavior
as the smaller population size, and MuSample has no significant deviation from this either.
Probability migration strategy now benefits from larger cluster sizes but still with diminishing
returns as the cluster size increases. Sample strategy however has a steeper gradient with
increasing cluster size, however looking at the Y-axis the overall change from 2 nodes to
32 nodes results in an improvement of about 1500 fitness evaluations out of approximately
11500.

LeadingOnes
In this section we investigate the properties for cGA on LeadingOnes with different mi-
gration strategies and topologies with varying population size, migration intervals, problems
Page 76 of 107

CGA
sizes and cluster sizes.
7.2.2.1
Population size test
For this experiment we ran different population sizes (K) in the range 1-400 in increments
of 1. This was averaged over 4096 runs for problem size 100. This section descries the results.
We found the optimal population size (K) to be 99 using 112.089,49 fitness evaluation
on average to solve the problem. Below a population size of around 50 cGA was no longer
able to solve the problem every time. For results see figure [138].
7.2.2.2
Migration interval Test
To investigate the properties of cGA with varying migration intervals (MI) on Leadin-
gOnes each test will be run for N = 100 with the value for K (Population size) as found in
the previous section and cluster size 16. The migration intervals that were tested are 1.5x,
x ∈[0; 20] and averaged over 256 runs. This section describes the results
NoMigration
First considering NoMigration where we do not allow any migration and
once one of the 16 nodes has found a solution all stop. This was averaged over 256 runs.
On average it took 68.439,55 (see table 63) fitness evaluations to find the optimal solution.
This will be used for comparison in later tests.
Complete topology
For Sample we see that it benefits from a low migration interval of
1 improving significantly on NoMigration with an average of 9.859,69(see table 64) max
fitness evaluations. The Sample strategy likely manage to improve on the solution as it
combats a problem with cGA on LeadingOnes where with some probability the best of the
two samples has a 0 on a low bit, despite the probability of sampling a 1 is quite high, and
the worst of the two samples has a 1 resulting on the probability of that bit being decreased.
This becomes more likely the further along cGA is in optimizing the problem. This is due
to the chance of a solved bit6 being sampled to 0 is at best 1 −1/N. With half the bits
solved we expect a solved bit to be flipped every second iteration and thus we expect both
solutions to have a solved bit flipped every 4th iteration. When this happens we are bound
to take a counter-productive step as long as the bits they flipped were different. The chance
of flipping a bit increases with the amount of solved bits and thus the frequency of counter-
productive steps increases. We see this especially as the amount of upper bound hits for
cGA on LeadingOnes is significantly lower than on OneMax. This becomes less likely if the
nodes all communicate and only the best set of samples is selected as a solved bit would have
to be flipped for all of them at once for the same to occur during migration. In figure [61]
we see that a lower migration interval for cGA on LeadingOnes lowers the largest amount of
lower bound hits seen throughout the run, and does not seem to stagnate at around 60 upper
bound hits as with NoMigration (see figure 139). This suggests that not only does it limit
the lower bound hits, possibly as a result of genetic drift, but also combats the previously
6With solved bit we mean a bit which probability is significantly increased to be one

described effect and allows for more upper bound hits, making progress more consistent
towards the end of the optimization process as seen from figure [142]. We observe that when
increasing the migration interval (MI) the performance of the Sample strategy converges on
that of NoMigration.

For MuSample we also see it benefiting from a low migration interval, with the optimal

the performance of NoMigration as MI increases.
For Probability migration strategy we see that a low migration interval leads to poor
performance, which could be caused by the frequent migration inhibiting the nodes from
progressing as progress made by one node might be averaged out after migration by the
rest of the cluster. We see from figure [62] that a lower migration interval of 5 has both a
lower amount of lower bound hits in the probability vector but also significantly lower upper
bound hits. It seems to stagnate at a lower amount of upper bound hits.

This suggests that this migration strategy does efficiently decrease the lower bound hits
as a result of genetic drift (see same graph for NoMigration, figure 139). It also suggests
the migration strategy inhibits progress, suggesting that with a low migration interval the
balance between progress and avoiding genetic drift does not constitute a good balance.
Interestingly this effect seems to influence how the algorithm progresses in terms of fitness
later in the optimization process as the fitness progression (fitness plotted against fitness
evaluations) has a similar gradient to the same algorithm with a migration interval of 20 for
the first 20000 fitness evaluations, however as the rate of progression decreases as the fitness
increases this has a significant effect on the total amount of fitness evaluations. See figure

Figure 63: For K = 99 and N = 100 a plot over MI (Migration interval) and the max amounts
of fitness evaluations done by a node in a cluster. This was averaged over 256 runs for cluster

KCircle topology
For the KCircle topology the Sample strategy has similar behavior
to the same strategy with the Complete topology, but does not benefit from the lower
connectivity, with the average max required fitness evaluations being 26.030,55(see table
64) compared to the 9.859,69(see table 64) of Complete. The bound hits of the probability
vector and fitness progression over fitness evaluations are similar to that of Complete (see
figure [145 and 146]]) however closer to that of NoMigration. Likely this is caused by the
lower connectivity resulting in each node receiving a smaller set of samples on migration,
and as seen from Complete topology, cGA with Sample migration benefits from selecting
the best sample from a large set for LeadingOnes. The diversity of having nodes in a cluster
receive and update the probability vector with different samples ever migration intuitively
does not benefit cGA on a problem like LeadingOnes as progress can always be made by
local searching i.e. flipping the "next" bit7. Interestingly Probability migration strategy
does not seem to be impacted by the lower connectivity, which suggests that each node does
not benefit significantly from receiving a larger set of probability vectors at each migration.
This is explained by the amount of probability vectors not being as important as the bits,
with genetic drift or erroneous low probability, being on different indices.
7By next bit we mean the bit on the next index in the bit-string
Page 79 of 107
==PAGE:86==
Master’s Project
7.2
CGA
Figure 64: For K = 99 and N = 100 a plot over MI (Migration interval) and the max amounts
of fitness evaluations done by a node in a cluster. This was averaged over 256 runs for cluster
size 16 (16 nodes per run). Each plot shows how the different migration strategies perform
with different MI’s
Any index with an erroneously lowered probability can have its probability increased
through probability migration. This can only occur when the migrated vectors do not have
an error on the same bit. If this holds true then the amount of received probability vectors
does not matter as they will always have a 10% influence on the probability vector. The
chance of it not holding true is proportional to the amount of vectors received. The amount
of influence any vector has decreases with the amount received. These two effects seemingly
cancel each other out thereby having no effect. All this explains why Probability migration
does not benefit from increased connectivity.
The optimal found migration interval for Probability migration is lower for KCircle
at 57 compared to 129 for Complete, suggesting that there is a benefit to more frequent
migration. Looking at the graph in figure 64 however, there is a very small gradient in the
interval 57-129, so this is not likely a significant observation.
MuSample benefits from having migration interval 1 similar to Sample, however does
not improve significantly on NoMigration as the average max fitness evaluations is less than
a standard deviation from that of NoMigration. The graph for varying migration intervals
does however have an interesting "hill" before than dropping off again and plateauing. It
is suspected that this is the result of the two effects of upper an lower probability bound
hits as the migration interval changes. Looking at figure [143] we see that as MI increases
they plateau at higher amount of lower bound hits, notice as MI increases, the difference in
the lower bound hits at the end of the graph increases less and less. Looking at the upper
bound hits and increase in MI conversely causes a plateau in upper bound hits to increase,
again with diminishing increases. We suspect that this balance causes an increase in the
required max fitness evaluations. The lower bound hits are suspected to be easier to flip
than to increase their probabilities as we see them flatten out. This means as MI increases the
algorithm has to do a bigger jump, flipping more bits at the same time. This growth appears
to cap out at MI = 30 while the growth of upper bound hits increases. This suggests that
for MI >= 30 the problem gets easier to jump as there is less chance of a solved bit flipping.
This would explain why at this point the amount of fitness evaluations start decreasing. We
suspect that this balance cause an increase in the required max fitness evaluations while
the lower bound hit count increases. To find the solution to the problem, the algorithm at
Page 80 of 107
==PAGE:87==
Master’s Project
7.2
CGA
the end has to flip increasingly many bits with the lower bound probability. However as Mi
increases, the change in lower bound hits stagnate up to about MI=30 and thus the increase
in fitness evaluations also stagnates. As MI increases past 30 the amount of migrations
that happen decrease and thus the negative effect and the amount of fitness evaluations
also decreases. These effects do seem to become insignificant at higher migration intervals
and we see the graph for MuSample plateau thereafter. The effect of the lower bound hit
plateau is also seen in figure 144 where the fitness progression is very similar with all the
different migration intervals until late in the optimization process, where they don’t deviate
in how much progress is made but the lower migration intervals are just more likely to flip
the remaining bits and stop. Note these effects are quite subtle, however the deviation in
fitness evaluations is also around a few percent.
Star topology
For Star topology, both MuSample and Probability migration does not
seem to have any significant effect on the performance, suggesting that one node does not
benefit sufficiently from receiving either samples or probability vectors from the remaining
nodes to cause any increase in performance. This might be because putting all the learning’s
from all the nodes into one node does not provide any value as the node who has the most
leading ones will likely have the best probability vector or generate the best samples and
there is no benefit in using the remaining ones.
However Sample strategy does manage to improve on NoMigration, which could be
caused by one node occasionally making a large improvement in fitness and sharing that
with the center node will then provide two nodes that are closer to the optimal solution and
thereby increasing the chance of finishing faster. This is improvement brings the average
required max fitness evaluations down to 53.404,31(see table 66) and is not as efficient as
both Sample and probability distribution strategy for both KCircle and Complete topology.

7.2.2.3
Variable N Test
To investigate the properties of cGA with the different topologies and migration strate-
gies we run tests with the migration intervals and populations size identified in the two
previous sections with varying problem sizes ranging from 20-500 in increments of 20. We
let the populations size scale with both N · log(N) · c where c is a constant found such that
for N = 100 the function equal 99 thereby matching the values for population size we found
earlier. Each test will be run with cluster size 16 and averaged over 256 runs. This section
describes the results.
Serial N Test
For LeadingOnes we see that cGA perform according to the theoretical
results, following a function of O(N 2 · log(N)) in figure 151. This will be used for later
comparison.
Complete
For Complete topology we see that the three migration strategies scale sim-
ilarly, but with some constant factor separating them. Sample migration strategy scales
significantly better with about a factor of 12 compared to the serial version. For results see

To test the scaling properties of cGA on LeadingOnes with the different migration
strategies and topologies in terms of cluster size we run each migration strategy with each
topology for cluster sizes 2-32 in powers of 2 and for torus we run with cluster sizes (quadratic
numbers) 4, 9, 16, 25 for each of the populations sizes and migration intervals found in the
previous sections. For this experiment we use problem size 100 and average over 256 runs.
This section describes the results.
Complete
For Complete topology all three migration strategies scales similarly to that
of NoMigration, yet separated by some constant factor.
Probability migration strategy
does seem to benefit more from an increased cluster size with a steeper gradient compared
NoMigration and the remaining strategies, however still with diminishing returns as the

cluster size increases. The Sample migration strategy manages to decrease the max fitness

7.2.3.1
Population size test
To find a suitable population size we tested population sizes from 1-400 in increments
of 1 and found the optimal population size to be 101 for N = 100 (N being problem size).
This was averaged over 1536 runs. Below a population size of around 50 not all runs solved
the problem. For results see figure 158 and table 75.
7.2.3.2
Migration interval Test
To investigate the effects of Migration on cGA tests were run with varying migration
intervals (MI) on TwoPeaks each test will be run for N = 100 with the value for K (Population
size) as found in the previous section and cluster size 16. The migration intervals that were
tested are 1.5x, x ∈[0; 20] and averaged over 256 runs. This section describes the results.
NoMigration
First considering NoMigration where we do not allow any migration and
once one of the 16 nodes has found a solution all stop. This was averaged over 250 runs. We
see that the average amount of largest fitness evaluations it takes is 75,593.56 (see table
76). This will be used for later comparisons.
Complete topology
For Sample migration strategy we see that it benefits from a low
migration interval of 1 with a large improvement managing to only need 11,237.26(see table
77) max fitness evaluations on average. This is similar to what we found for LeadingOnes.
This also helps all nodes align (Align in the sense that they are all working on LeadingOnes or
TrailingZeros) such that at every migration they do not work against each other by sending
samples that will be counterproductive the problem they are trying to optimize eg. A sample
from a node working on LeadingOnes would not help a node working on TrailingZeros.
As the migration interval increases the performance approaches that of NoMigration,
which suggests that the impact of receiving a sample from a node working on a different
problem if rare enough does not cause significant drops in performance.
For MuSample we see it does not manage to improve on NoMigration contrary to what
was seen for LeadingOnes. This is likely as MuSample does not manage to align the nodes,
and the frequent migration cases the nodes to work against one another. This is intuitively
different from Sample strategy as Sample will have all nodes pick the same Sample. This
ensures that all nodes work on the same problem at each migration. However MuSample
can again be understood as a collective average step which could consist of half of the nodes
working on LeadingOnes and the rest on TrailingZeros which would not help them align,
however hinder their progression. As the migration interval increases we see that it stops
having a significant impact and approaches that of NoMigration. Figure 160 provides a
closer look at low migration intervals. At migration interval 1 the performance is closer to
that of NoMigration. This could indicate that they align, perhaps as a result of the chance
of an inequality in the amount of nodes working on the two problems. This will similar to
a majority vote cause a push towards working on the problem with more nodes. The spike
might then be a the result of a balancing act as slightly higher migration interval will not
allow them to align and they now sabotage each other at each migration.
Page 85 of 107
==PAGE:92==
Master’s Project
7.2
CGA
For Probability migration strategy we have a similar balance between aligning the nodes
and not causing them to work against each other. We see that as the migration interval
increases the performance gets worse, likely as a result of a similar effect where the nodes
are no longer able to align and now work against each other. This peak is shifted right and
larger than seen for MuSample, likely as Probability migration strategy has a larger impact
on the receiving nodes compared to MuSample. Probability migration strategy does not get
as good results as seen for MuSample at migration interval 38 (the found optimal) however
this is expected based on the results for LeadingOnes. Based on figure 69 it seems that with
a sufficiently high migration interval the performance would approach that of NoMigration,
and Probability migration strategy does not seem to be beneficial for Complete topology on
TwoPeaks with cGA.

KCircle topology
For KCircle topology we see similar behavior for Sample and similar to
LeadingOnes the Sample migration strategy does not benefit from the lower connectivity of
KCircle. It still seems that the nodes can align w.r.t. the problem they are optimizing as the
performance is not inhibited by the migration so we imagine the working effects to be similar
to the ones for LeadingOnes. Worth noting is how the nodes align. For Complete since all
nodes are connected to all, we imagine that they all align to work on the same problem.
For KCircle however it might be possible for them to align in groups of 2 or more nodes
as groups of connected nodes of this size and greater will have at least the same internal
connectivity as external and the group might therefore have the ability to deal with the
incoming information without being too hindered in performance. This might also explain
the worse performance for KCircle compared to Complete as if these groups exist and are
surrounded by nodes who are working on a different problem they would hinder progress at
each migration. Aligning smaller groups in the cluster might also be easier and more likely.
For MuSample we see in figures 70 and 160 that the lower connectivity has reduced
the spike in fitness evaluations at low migration intervals. As the performance is still worse
when NoMigration, it indicates that they still do not manage to frequently enough align all
nodes to benefit from this migration strategy. However the lower spike might be explained
by the lower connectivity meaning each node receiving samples each migration which might
Page 86 of 107
==PAGE:93==
Master’s Project

CGA
make the probability update made more meaningful to fitness progression. This might be
combined with some probability of aligning in smaller groups where they don’t work against

is worse than that of NoMigration, it still seems unlikely that the majority manage to align,
similarly one should keep the performance of MUSample on leading ones in mind as that
problem did not benefit a lot from this migration sample an in turn TwoPeaks is unlikely to
either.
For Probability migration we observe similar behavior where a lower migration interval
is more likely to be able to align the nodes, and similarly at migration interval 1 for Complete
topology the performance is not as good as that of NoMigration as seen for LeadingOnes.
The lower connectivity of KCircle also seems to influence how counterproductive migration
between nodes that are not aligned are as the peak in figure 70 is shifted left and down
compared to Complete topology, suggesting that at a lower migration interval the nodes can
no longer align and the impact of migration between these nodes is less counterproductive
compared to Complete. The found optimal migration interval is 3325 and likely higher as
the performance approaches that of NoMigration when the migration interval is sufficiently
high.

align at that migration interval and about half the time you would expect the two nodes to try
and optimize different problems and the migration will then hurt performance significantly.
Once the cluster size becomes large enough we see that it solves all runs. The decrease in
fitness evaluations might be explained by a larger chance of a node aligning with its two
neighbors by chance. When a groups is aligned its center nodes are protected from the
negative impact of migration by a factor proportional to the distance to the aligned groups
external edges.
Figure 77: For K = 101 and N = 100 a plot over the cluster size and the max amounts of
fitness evaluations done by a node in a cluster. This was averaged over 256 runs. Each plot
shows how the compact genetic algorithm performed with varying cluster sizes for each of
the migration strategies for the KCircle topology
Star
For the Star topology we do not see any improvement on NoMigration in terms
of scaling, and all migration strategies performs similar to that of NoMigration. This is
expected based on the results of the migration interval tests.

7.2

7.2.4
JumpK
This section covers our tests for CGA on the JumpK problem. We picked a problem size
of 100, and varied the size of the jump to increase the difficulty. When choosing population
size we also test multiple sizes of the jump.
7.2.4.1
CGA on JumpK
For the sake of figuring out how cGA worked on jumpK when the fitness reaches n −k
we tried to see how the probability vector changes over time. For this we ran a smaller
c# version of the cGA algorithm that would run 1000 runs simultaneously, then order and
average out their probability vectors. This version used a jump size of 5 and a problem size
of 100. When a run succeeded in finding the optimal fitness we did not stop, but did not let
it update the probability vector. We also added Upper and Lower bounds of (n −1)/n and
1/n.
The purpose of this was to simulate the cases where it doesn’t solve at all. In figure 80
we can see the result. from the left graph we gather that some bits suffer from genetic drift.
In the middle graph we can tell that as more bits get randomly chosen, all probabilities go
up while we also see some of the probabilities maxing out. On the right we can see how if
the algorithm does not solve the problem it increases variance. This lowers its probability
of success. At iteration 5000, we found the chance of success to be 0.000045. If instead we
averaged the probabilities out to all be 0.93488 we would get a success rate of 0.001190, which
is 26 times larger. This phenomenon of increasing variance, we also like to call reinforcing
what its learned. Its learned that some bits need to be 0 and thus it makes the probability
for some bits extremely low, decreasing its chances of success.
Looking at figure 81 we can see how the highest probability bit, quickly maxes out,
while the lowest probability bit tends to suffer from genetic drift the first 250 iterations
before being relevant for OneMax and when the overall probability vector averages over 95
1’s it starts decreasing.
For how Probability changes when jump is 50 so theres no OneMax part see figure 165
and 166. At the 50000th iteration the probability of success is also 1.346039185 ∗10−88
compared to 7.888609052 ∗10−31 if all probabilities were 50%.
Page 95 of 107
==PAGE:102==
Master’s Project

CGA

On the left we can see progress in early iterations. In the middle we can see as it starts
hitting the upper bound. On the right how extended run-time leads to higher variance

guarantee success on the problem with the first time we got 256/256 successes being at
k = 211 when on the graph it should be around k = 70 −90. Instead we went with jump
size 4 which got 256/256 at k = 86.

7.2.4.3
Migration interval
To choose migration interval we ran the mi value starting at 1 and multiplying by 1.5
for each test. This continued up until mi = 3325 where the amount of iterations between
migration surpassed the amount of iterations on average to solve serially. All the tests were
done with 16 nodes.
Complete
In figure 83 we can see how Sample does very well for low migration, but
quickly hits its peak.
MuSample does negatively, but closer to NoMigration as mi goes
up and Probability migration does well for low migration, not as good as Sample, but the
amount of fitness evaluations climbs slower with mi than it does for Sample. In figure 84
we can see how each of the migration strategies progress fitness over time with a migration.
One might notice that it looks like the fitness at some point is between 96 and 100. This is
due to averaging out the fitness in this area as more and more of the runs complete around
here.
Figure 83: A plot of how many fitness evaluations it took for UMDA to solve the jumpK
problem when varying migration interval for the complete topology
For Sample we don’t expect any improvement on completing the jump, as we know
the bits to be self-reinforcing and diversity to be low with Sample migration on Complete
topology. Instead we expect the reason for its lower amount of fitness evaluations to be its
performance of the OneMax part of the problem. This is also what we see in figure 84.
Page 97 of 107
==PAGE:104==
Master’s Project
7.2
CGA
MuSample does as well as NoMigration for the OneMax part of the Problem (see figure
84), but seemingly does worse at the Jump part. We know the bits to be self-reinforcing,
but in the context of MuSample the chance of a negative change meant for one of the lower
probability bits to hit a higher bit is much more likely than the reverse. This then leads
to small decreases in fitness and a worse chance of solving the problem. We also know that
extending the time the algorithm runs gives a worse chance of completing the jump.
Lastly Prob utilizes high diversity to increase the chances of success. It distributes the
overall probability sum over the n bits to increase the chance of n 1’s. This also helps with
the OneMax part of the problem as seen in figure 84.
Figure 84: A plot of how many fitness evaluations it took for UMDA to solve the jumpK
problem when varying migration interval for the complete topology
Torus
See figure 167 for Torus topology results. It can be observed that lower connectivity
increase the fitness evaluations for Sample. This is expected to be explained by the OneMax
part of the problem being solved slower.
Circle
See figure 168 for Circle topology results. Here it can be seen that the pattern
continues as Sample does even worse than other topologies.
Star
For star the differences between strategies is very minor, but it can still be seen that
MuSample makes a negative impact and Sample a positive one. We assume its due to the
same reasons as for other topologies, but now it only affects the center node.
7.2.4.4
Problem size
To focus on the difficulty of the jump we have chosen to vary the size of said jump and
see how success rate and time taken is affected without varying the amount of bits in the
bitstring. For the experiment the problem size went from 2 to 6 running all combination of
topologies and migration strategies for each value 256 times. The experiment was also done
with NoMigration to have something to compare to. Nomigration started failing at jump
size 6 with 254/256 successes.
Complete
See figure 85. For complete Sample does well for small jump sizes but seems to
scale worse than probability. This seems to come from Sample not being able to help with
the jump part of the problem while Jumpk increases the odds of completing the jump.

Success Rate
This section will discuss the discoveries from table 88. We mainly look at
the jump size 8-10 columns as these holds the most interesting information.
The first thing to notice is MuSample rarely completing. The second thing to notice
is Sample does improve success rate, even if we expect it to run slower than No-Migration.
We presume this comes from the increased time it has to solve the jump since it solves
OneMax faster. Another point is that we limit the algorithms runtime on iterations, but
do not count migration rounds as iterations. This means that Sample gets twice as many
fitness evaluations as No-Migration and probability migration.
Probability migration does better than all other migration strategies getting 63/256
success rate on Torus. The second best migration strategy is Complete Sample with about a
fourth of the success rate. At jump size 9 the proportion between them is about 3.6 and at
size 8 its 1.8 though comparing to Torus Sample instead gives 1.7. Torus is also interesting
as its connectivity gives a good combination of diversity and exterior connectivity within
groups which should lead to higher success rates. For e.g. Probability migration it can be
observed that the Torus topology gives slightly better rates, but comparing the 64/256 to
58/256 of Torus-Prob and Circle-Prob on jump size 10, gives rather high p-values of over
0.6 using both the Fisher test and the Chi Squared test, meaning we can not reject the idea
their probabilities are the same. The same goes for comparing Torus-Prob to Complete-Prob
with p-values of over 0.2.

Torus we use 4, 9, 16, 25, 36 to ensure even distribution of nodes. Each experiment was run
256 times.
Complete

we found for OneMax, so we suspect it simply solves the OneMax part better. If we had
more time increasing jump size could give a better idea of scaling with jump.

To evaluate cGA on the MaxSat problem we have used the source of instances described

with. We found the for cGA a good level of complexity was 100 literals with 430 clauses,
and selected instance 4, 5, and 2 as they provide a simple instance with all solved, and two
gradually more complex instances with fewer solved. We with a gradually more complete
search (population size intervals going towards 1) found that for MaxSat cGA generally
prefers low population size of around 5-8, and below we see the optimal found for each of
the select instances.
