7.2.5.3
Migration interval tests
To investigate the properties of cGA with varying migration intervals (MI) on MaxSat
each test will be run for the three instances selected with 100 literals and 430 clauses with
the values for K (Population size) as found in the previous section and cluster size 16. The
migration intervals that were tested are 1-262,144 in powers of 2 and averaged over first 50
runs where-after all configurations that deviated less than three standard deviations from
the best found migration interval (in terms of fitness evaluations) for a given configuration
in a given instance were run at least 750 times. This section describes the results.
Complete
Looking at the plots for instance 5 and 2 seen in figure 176 and 177 we see that
none of the migration strategies manages to significantly improve on the amount of fitness
evaluations required to solve the problem. It seems that with a higher migration interval
the performance worsens where-after it plateaus around the performance of NoMigration as
the migration interval increases. We expect this to be caused by the migration strategies
and cGA considering these bits to be independent as they have been for most previous
Page 102 of 107
==PAGE:109==
Master’s Project
7.2
CGA
problems, however this is not the case for MaxSat and sharing samples and probabilities
without sharing any found dependency might not have a positive impact on the progression
and fitness. For this problem multivariate EDA’s might be more beneficial.
We do see an improvement for instance 4 (which is a simple instance by how many fitness
evaluations it takes to solve it) as seen in figure 175 and table 91. The three migration
strategies all seem to have poor performance when the migration interval is too low, but
with the right balance they need less fitness evaluations to find the optimal solution. We see
that both MuSample and Sample strategy benefit from a quite low migration interval of 8
with Sample strategy only needing 22,099.11 fitness evaluations on average. Too frequent
migration might cause all nodes to explore the search space collectively (as we have seen
that to be an effect of these migration strategies) which is not likely beneficial for a problem
like MaxSat where exploration likely is beneficial. We have also seen that aggressive (low)
migration is beneficial when you can always find progression close in the local search space
as in OneMax and LeadingOnes, but one could imagine that in MaxSat it is often necessary
to briefly accept a worse fitness to escape a local minima. This however might be inhibited
by frequently migration using Sample migration strategy as all deciding on the best set of
Samples might not allow nodes to select a accept a worse solution temporarily.
For Probability migration strategy we observe performance similar to Sample strategy
but with a higher migration interval of 128. Similarly too frequent migration might prevent
nodes from exploring worse solutions and when sharing the probability vector this might have
a larger impact which would explain the higher optimal migration interval. Interestingly
Probability migration strategy might be able to share the dependencies of bits as any found
relation from one node can now be communicated through the probability vector.
KCircle
For instance 4 we see from the plot in figure [178] similar behavior as with Com-
plete topology, however with the lower connectivity of KCircle we see that the found optimal
migration interval of MuSample and Sample migration strategy is lower at 4 compared to 8
as found with Complete topology. The average largest fitness evaluations required has also
decreased a (See table [92]).
This suggests that the lower connectivity of KCircle allows for more exploration while
having more frequent migrations compared to Complete topology. This is due to the effect
of having frequent migrations that normally align all nodes, only aligns groups in KCircle.
This might explain the improvement in fitness evaluations.
For Probability migration strategy the found optimal migration interval has not changed
from 128.
Yet improvements in max fitness evaluations can be observed which suggests
that it is beneficial to for all nodes to not receive the same set of probability vectors at
each migration.
We again believe that this might foster some diversity and exploration
that is beneficial when solving MaxSat. With the Mann-Whitnes test giving a p-value of
1.454596 · 10−5 (see table [100]) Probability migration strategy for Complete and KCircle
topology are statistically significantly different.
For instance 5 and 2 we again see no improvements as a result of the three migration
strategies likely for similar reasons as discussed for Complete topology as we observe similar
behavior, see figures 180 and 179. This implies that the migration strategies considered
Page 103 of 107
==PAGE:110==
Master’s Project
on cGA for more complex MaxSat instances are not beneficial and does not significantly
improve on fitness evaluations.
Star
For the Star topology we see no significant improvement on any of the instances. This
might be a result of one node receiving all information from the other nodes that might be
exploring completely different areas of the search space being to chaotic and not resulting in
any beneficial learnings which in turn improves fitness. This might however also be caused by
the benefit that the center node receives not having a large enough influence to meaningfully
impact the amount of fitness evaluations. For data see figures 181, 182 and 183.
Torus
For Torus we see that the Sample migration strategy performs similar to what we
saw with KCircle also with a migration interval of 4. However Probability migration strategy
performs similar to what was seen in with Complete, which might suggest that the benefit
of a lower connectivity for this strategy might have a cutoff such that whether each node
receives 4 probability vectors (as with Torus) or 16 (as with Complete) does not matter in
terms of being more exploratory. It might also be explained by how information propagates
through the topology, as we have seen for KCircle it takes N/2 migrations for information
to make it from one node to the one furthest away and less for both Torus and Complete.
The MuSample migration strategy performs worse than what was seen in both KCircle and
Complete. For results see figures [186], [184], [185], and table [93].
8
Conclusion
In conclusion this report has covered the insights into how the island model and migra-
tion affects EDA’s.
A modular framework for conducting experiments in a high performance compute clus-
ter was designed and implemented. The framework allowed for experimentation on a variety
of different topologies, migration strategies and EDA’s on a large scale. This together with
the design of a workflow for conducting and evaluationg experiments as well as the imple-
mentation of a data parsing framework, accomplished the goals of this project related to the
software design.
Evaluating OneMax the island models showed great improvement for both UMDA and
CGA. From UMDA it was observed how the timing of migrations was important as doing
migrations when little progress was done by the algorithm led to little value of said migration.
From cGA we saw the island models’ effect on genetic drift. The two migration strategies
MuSample and Probability migration improved the running time significantly by reducing
the chance of genetic drift. They also made the effects of genetic drift worse as they reduced
the chance of getting rid of it.
Evaluating UMDA on LeadingOnes it was discovered that MuSample could have nega-
tive effects on the progress as a whole as negative progress on the fitness would be averaged
out to all nodes. The same effect is observed for Probability migration, but the β value
reduced the negative effects. For cGA the LeadingOnes problem is notoriously difficult due
Page 104 of 107
==PAGE:111==
Master’s Project
to only sampling two points, but using the Sample migration strategy the chance of negative
project is severely reduced.
The TwoPeaks problem gave insight into alignment and how a low migration interval
greatly increases the chances of this. It was also observed that the issues of unaligned, also
known as diverse nodes can be mitigated through high migration interval as the decrease in
fitness happens more rarely.
The JumpK problem gave insight into the chance of escaping traps decreasing the longer
an EDA has spend in it. This can as observed be counteracted through diversity and mi-
gration strategies that average out progress between the nodes (MuSample and Probability
migration).
Lastly MaxSat was observed to benefit from diversity and migration strategies that
average out progress as escaping local maxima and covering a large part of the search space
are the two most important factors for solving it with a Univariate EDA.
For cGA a general pattern regarding the effect of Probability migration was observed
where the size of changes on migration heavily outweighed the size of changes on a normal
iteration. Frequent migration could thus hold back the algorithm from progressing, but also
that migration could give greater benefits.
Overall the benefits of island models seem prominent in EDAs, given the right migra-
tion strategies, topologies and migration intervals. For most simple problems Sample does
best on UMDA and can also greatly benefit cGA on slower progressing problems such as
LeadingOnes. For complex problems with traps MuSample or Probability migration help
escape traps as long as the model holds sufficient diversity. Migration interval affects the
diversity and alignment, but also how often it benefits from migration. For problems that
value alignment this causes a balance to be nesecary. Lastly the topology should for complex
problems be balanced in terms of connectivity as aligned nodes progress faster but diversified
nodes can better escape traps both of which can be achieved through groups in a topology
with low enough connectivity.
9
Further Works
This section covers interesting aspects of the topics we’ve worked with that could be
dived deeper into. This is aspects that either aren’t covered by the scope of this report, we
didn’t have time to cover as well as phenomenons we found interesting. It is structured as a
list of general topics one could investigate in the future.
Population size with island model configuration
In this report our experiments for
each algorithm and problem started with a test to find the population size best fit for running
serially. That population size has then been used for all configurations of our island model.
We have no good argument as to why the optimal population size for serial running should
be the same as for the island models so experiments on the effects of island model on the
optimal population size could be interesting.
Page 105 of 107
==PAGE:112==
Master’s Project
Scaling migration interval
Same as with the population size when we had found an
optimal migration interval for running on a specific problem size and a specific cluster size,
that interval was reused for all problem sizes and cluster sizes. Testing whether the optimal
migration interval changes for different problem sizes and cluster sizes could be interesting,
especially for the configurations where it wasn’t a matter of "pick MI = 1" or "don’t
migrate".
Probability vector over time
For JumpK on cGA we did a small simulation showing
how the probability vector looked at certain iterations. This gave good insight into how cGA
worked on JumpK, and thus changing our code to also give us the probability vector could
give better insight into how the two algorithms worked on specific problems. This could
further be used to see how the island model also affects the probability vector.
Connectivity
In this report we have tried to infer certain details about connectivity by
using three different topologies with differing levels of connectivity. We cannot say exactly
where the three topologies are compared to each other in connectivity due to their differences.
We suggest using CircleK/RingK to vary the connectivity, increasing K until the Complete
topology. Doing this would give a more controlled variance in connectivity.
Problem Size JumpK
For the JumpK problem a lot of the theory analysed the running
time to be tied to the problem size when the jump size was also tied to the problem size.
Delving deeper into how the island model compares to the theory could be interesting. To
do so one would have to vary jump size and population size together.
New Algorithms and variants
UMDA and cGA are a small group of algorithms to have
experimented on. They both have many variants e.g. frequency cGA and many other EDAs
exist e.g. PBIL. More types of EDA’s also exist such as Bi-variate and Multi-variate. Testing
on algorithms that work in different ways will lead to more discoveries in the performance
and workings of the island model and its many configurations.
Topologies and Migration strategies
The topologies and migration strategies chosen
for this report were picked to cover different connectivity and how well diversity in the system
was expected to hold.
Many other topologies exist and there are many more migration
strategies. For Sample migration on cGA we chose to take the samples as pairs and pick the
one with the highest fitness. One could also see all samples as one big population and then
pick a pair of the worst and best, best and second best, worst and second worst and so on.
All of these possible topologies and migration strategies could identify new patterns in how
the different EDA’s react to migration. Testing topologies with disconnected island groups,
trees and varying connectivity across the topology could give insights into how groups form,
align and benefit the overall cluster. Working with a weighted migration strategy could
combine the benefits of Sample with that of MuSample/Probability migration.
Page 106 of 107
==PAGE:113==
Master’s Project
UMDA µ λ ratio.
In this report the ratio between µ and λ has been set at the very
beginning to a theoretical value found found in the literature for serial running. This ratio
might change with different configurations of topology, migration strategy and interval. With
more nodes and diversity therefrom a more elitist version of the algorithm might be optimal.
Phenomena
While analysing the experiments, we identified several phenomena which
were difficult to explain and could not be researched in time.
Looking into these could
reveal insights into the workings of island models on EDA’s.
Combine different configurations
We’ve found certain configurations to be beneficial
for certain parts of different problems. For example Sample migration on UMDA solved
OneMax in record time. Making a topology where certain groups run different algorithms/-
configurations could make an optimization algorithm that is beneficial on all problems.
Theoretical background for findings
Over the course of this project several discoveries
have been made, not all of which are theoretically proven. While we have backed up our the-
ories with statistical tests and further experiments, a theoretical analysis of the phenomena
would help ensure the intuitions we’ve gathered.
